		<shortDescription>Budget cutbacks but still effective</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Day of the Dead has been looked upon by alot of horror fans as being inferior to Romero's earlier works--Night of the Living Dead and his epic masterpiece..Dawn of the Dead. DayOTD is definately the darkest and most bleak out of the trilogy. A very closterphobic feel and an overall feeling of helpessness looms in the atmosphere as the zombie masses outweigh the humans by the odds of 400,000 to 1. While being stationed in an underground missle Silo, a team of Scientists and a Military squadron try and come up with a solution to eradicate the problem. Tensions flare and the difference in opinions on how the situation should be handled is finally the boiling point. Each person goes in different directions--pulling themselves apart from everyone else--defying ideas others offer to try and help. This film is not a very far cry to how the world has spun itself out of control. This was a sign of the times back in the mid 80's as the Reagan/Bush administration influenced Military power and the dreaded doom of chemical warfare was on the verge of destroying the world. This just proves to show that when people pull apart and spin in different directions, Problems are inevitable and solutions are halted. The gore in this film is almost theraputic to a certain degree--constructed by Tom Savini and his even MORE experienced F/X team. While this film is talky throughout, this is crucial to the plot. Romero's original vision for DayOTD was drastically shortened by budget constraints, but nonetheless he delivers a satisfying theraputic film that will always remain timeless.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Superlative, psychologically real, terrifying</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Disappointed to see so many naysayers reviewing this film, which I consider to be a classic, and potentially the best of the Romero "Dead" trilogy.The Romero films are typified by disturbingly realistic treatments of a fantastical, and now cliched, concept - the undead. There is an almost neo-realist feel to Romero films, with their commonplace settings, even pace, and extremely sophisticated scripts."Day of the Dead" throws the viewer straight into a world where the undead cover the earth. From the opening shot where survivors land a helicopter in a metropolitan area to find it completely swamped, to the undergound military installation with its personal abattoir of the undead - the atmosphere throughout is relentless and bleak.The characters are ruined people, who suffer from the extreme psychological conditions of their oppressive environment.Romero puts the story first, and so the film is dialogue heavy. But this is a good thing - and more like a Scorcese film than a Hammer horror.If you're an intelligent, well-versed cinema fan who happens to enjoy disturbing films, then SEE THIS MOVIE.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Flawed, but don't be so fast to dismiss it.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Day of the Dead" has provided me with one of my earliest horror-related memories. When I was six years old, my local videostore had a poster on display for the video release. There was Bub the Zombie in all his rotting glory. "The Darkest Day of Horror the World Has Ever Known!" screamed the tagline. Needless to say, this striking image captivated my innocent little mind. However, I knew there was no way my parents would let me watch it. So the issue was pretty much dead for awhile. Still, even when the poster came down, I still would remember it whenever I went there. (The store has long since closed shop, thanks to the rise of superchains like Blockbuster.)It has been a good sixteen years later, and I finally rented the damn thing. I was aware of the negative reviews it recieved, even among some horror fans. However, it's actually a pretty decent film with some good moments, although it does have some serious flaws. Romero did what he could under the circumstances. A troubled production, the film went through hasty rewrites after investors slashed the budget when he refused to promise delivery of an R-rated movie.The final result is certainly worth at least one viewing. I was intrigued by the idea of a zombie who has not yet lost all semblance of humanity. "Bub the zombie" actually generates sympathy for his plight. It is a grim reminder that zombies are victims too. They were once human like us, but have been reduced to mockeries of life.Lori Cardille delivers a solid performance, in the mold of the Alien series' Ellen Ripley. She shines as a rock of sanity in a sea of madness. However, Joseph Pilato overacts terribly as an unstable military leader, although I suspect he enjoyed himself in the role.I admit the midsection is a bit of a drag. It's more than compensated for by the gleefully gruesome finale, as people are torn apart by the walling undead. Makeup artist Tom Savini displays some of his best work here. His zombies are the most convincing in the series thus far. Some of the zombies in previous "Dead" films looked a bit fake, but here they actually look like real walking corpses. Pure, 24-karat gold.Bring on "Twilight of the Dead," I say.**1/2 (out of ****)Released by United Film Distribution</fullReview>

		<fullReview>A lot of people look at Day of the Dead and completely cast it away as being a poor film solely on the fact that it's not as good as the previous Dawn of the Dead. If you're a true fan of zombie films you'll be able to appreciate Day of the Dead for what it is, don't be blinded by the fact that it's a sequel to an amazing film which was a lot better. It has it's good points, and it's definitely entertaining.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Unfair comparisons to Alien 3...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'm fairly sick of hearing how "Day of the Dead" is just as poor as "Alien 3" ("Alien 3" REALLY sucked). Yes, it isn't a movie that would appeal to everyone, but it has enough brilliant moments to qualify as a good horror movie. The acting is certainly hit-and-miss, with Lori Cardille and Joe Pilato making understandable and believable adversaries and the rest of the cast basically chewing scenery (except for Dr. Logan). The first scene is post-apocalyptically cool, and the mood is continued pretty effectively. Realistic zombie effects and modern automatic weapons make for a slightly grittier, far less surreal rendering of the world of the dead. The story is probably the film's greatest weak point; it lacks the epicness of "Dawn of the Dead" and the tension of "Night of the Living Dead," and what remains is somewhat overblown conflict between humans. In spite of all these problems, "Day" is still worth a rental for horror fans.</fullReview>

		<date>2002-01-25</date>
		<username>Jim Griffin (hubbahubbageek@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Good ideas buried under layers of cack. (Spoilers)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There's a noticeable similarity between George Romero's Dead trilogy and the later Alien franchise. Both series start with tense, claustrophobic films that quietly scare the audience with a degree of subtlety; both expand on their ideas for their first sequels, placing the same principles in a warlike setting and upping the action considerably; and, sadly, the pattern continues into the third instalments, because, like Alien 3, Day of the Dead is a hefty anti-climax. As the movie opens, the living dead are still munching their way through a thinning crop of humans, and this instalment is the most desolate, with its small group of characters speculating as to whether or not they are the last survivors on earth. It's a promising start that raises hopes that Dawn of the Dead's apocalyptic atmosphere will be continued, and further hope is found in the basic plot that sees scientists in an underground bunker attempting to find a solution to the zombie problem. It's a shame that these fresh ideas and fresh perspectives, which could have made a classic movie and a classic trilogy, are wasted on a film that lacks the intelligence, class and logic of its predecessors. It's a real disappointment. I'm not often impressed by horror, and I'm put off by the genre's tendency to churn out needless and endless sequels, so it was great to discover the intelligence and invention of the first two movies in the series. But Day of the Dead falls into the horror pitfalls that its predecessors had wisely avoided. Where there was invention there is now clich, where there were solid characters there are now cardboard cut-outs, and where there was tension there is now boredom and twiddling of thumbs.While the acting in the first two movies was nothing to write to the Academy about, here it nosedives past the quality found in TV movies, plummets beneath am-dram, and lands a few hundred miles below amateur porn. Joseph Pilato's performance in particular is so hammy that, if he followed the De Niro school of method acting, he must have spent months being salted, smoked and cured in preparation for the part. At best, Pilato's co-stars manage to make their underwritten and over-clichd characters look two dimensional. From the gung-ho military men to the crazy scientist, from the drunk Irishman to the platitude-spouting West-Indian who wants nothing from life but to lie on a beach, they have none of the complexities you would expect from having seen their predecessors. To add insult to these critical injuries, the dialogue they're saddled with is repetitive and banal. A great strength of the first two films was their moral ambiguity, but here it's ignored in favour of outright stupidity. When I watched this instalment, it was introduced by critic Mark Kermode, who discussed its ambiguity at length, insisting it allowed the audience to choose whether to be disgusted by the scientists or the military or the living dead, seeing that each was evil in their own way. Unless he's travelled to a mirrored universe where reality is inverted and this movie has a goatee, I'll have to say he's talking out rubbish. There is no doubt as to who we should be cheering for. The bad guys are ridiculously bad, managing to be sexist, racist and murderous, and the worst suffers by far the most grisly death.Strangely, it's the gore of the death scenes that comes out as the highlight of the movie. Night of the Living Dead had scared us without showing much, and it was to its credit, but here Romero takes the opposite approach without betraying his earlier work. Sadly, it's the only part of the film for which that can be said, but the effects are remarkable and worthy of the highest praise. Unfortunately, these scenes also show up a flaw that the first two movies had mostly managed to escape; the inconsistent strength of the zombies, that sees them struggling to push through a chain-link fence one minute, and happily ripping a body apart the next.Day of the Dead is a huge anti-climax, and in every sense a wasted opportunity. There are great ideas here, and great potential for an old idea to take off in a new direction, but they are lost in a film that has too little in the way of imagination and too much in the way of running time. If Night of the Living Dead was the fine starter, and Dawn of the Dead the filling main course, Day of the Dead is the turd Romero squeezed out the morning after.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A Day That Will Live In Infamy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'm going to have to say that after the phenomenal one-two punch of "Night of the Living Dead" and "Dawn of the Dead", George A. Romero's "Day of the Dead" is a disappointing finale to the trilogy. What's really frustrating is that there's little tastes of how awesome this movie could've been- like the opening scene, where four members of a government team land in a deserted city looking for survivors of the apocalyptic zombie invasion, but instead find only an army of the walking dead (including the excellent "Dr. Tongue" puppet). But then the film heads underground, where it focuses on the endless conflict between the scientists and military personnel. These scenes soon grow tedious- the tension is well established early on, but then it's repeatedly displayed in endless scenes of characters yelling and pointing guns at each other. All the soldiers are hateful and cruel and the scientists are bland (expect the mad Dr. Logan, who's bizarre experiments are a high-light of the movie). The best characters are the helicopter pilot and the alcoholic communications guy, who remain neutral for most of the film. Overall, the most likable and sympathetic character in the film is Bub, a zombie being "trained" by Logan. Howard Sherman's great performance is enhanced by Savini's excellent, expressive make-up. In fact, "Day" does feature the coolest-looking zombies of the three films, and the gore is fantastic, especially the finale of zombie mayhem. The actors are fine, especially Joe Pilato (from "Dawn"), who makes a great (if one-dimensional) villain, and Greg Nictero (the "N" of KNB effects) and Taso Stavrakis (a biker in "Dawn") appear as pot-smoking soldiers. The script's dialog is snappy, but the film's score is pretty terrible. So anyhoo, I guess this movie is better than no zombie movie, but makes me long for the masterpiece that is "Dawn of the Dead". Perhaps one day Romero will get the budget he deserves to make his ultimate view of a society decimated by the walking dead.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>"Out of Respect for the Dead"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"DAY OF THE DEAD," ahh, the final horrifying chapter in famed horror master's trilogy of deadly Anubus inspired terror. Critically, Romero had to know he wouldn't top himself with "NIGHT OF THE LIVING DEAD," but he did. He surpassed even his own expectations and the critics by making a sort of B+ film classic with DAWN OF THE DEAD now immortalized as a cult favorite that we all enjoy because it's such a poignant, satirical view of how 'zombified' society really is! Is also had the true horror and situational distress that its predecessor carried - people, pitted against each other to survive. And this is the situation for "DAY OF THE DEAD" Everyone knows the plot so I won't bother telling it, but think about the film rationally (from a writer's viewpoint). The world has been literally turned upside down. The dead control the world, and the living live like worms beneath the earth in what one character referred to as a 'great big 14-mile tombstone'. And that couldn't have been put more perfectly. As far as tension, hey, this film EXCELS in pitted situational stress and psychological mayhem. These people are in a Pandora's box. They're rats in a cage who're running out of supplies and who's world is becoming more and more claustrophobic as time continues. Now look at the characters; you've got Capt. Rhodes (Pilato) who's no more than a dog who's been told to 'sit!' but is getting antsy since that's it and that's all. He has to play leader, father and ruler to these people so they don't screw up the world he's living in anymore than it already is. My personal favorite character is Dr. 'Frankenstein' Logan (Liberty) always covered in blood-soaked scrubs and as jittery as the fly. Here's a man who's so lonely he's become best friends with a dead man. And so deranged because he's searching for a solution to an equation for which he has no control over. He might as well be trying to round off Pi. Critics who think this film is an inflammatory romp or overacted waste have their petty reasons since (yes) some dialogue is unreasonably profane and silly - but I doubt they realize that was written as part of the ridicule of the situation, which is why Sarah (Cardyle) is the focal character. These people are at war with a nation of zombies - it's a 'hopeless situation strategically' as Logan explains in a long-faced eulogy to the last members of the human race. 'Do you really think you can shoot them all?' he asks them, answering his own question, but also ignoring it. Bottom line... this is a fine horror film. For gore, for zombies, yes. I give this film an 8.5 for psychological terror, whereas (for example) a film like DEAD CALM would be a 10.0. Romero did a fine job on this, and he proved that three is not a crowd, it's a trilogy of terror. Until next time... Off the rock, on the ball, and strictly low-key...</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>The last day of some, the continuing agony of others..</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Day of the Dead (1985) is the latest in my collection, and, I watched Zombie (1979, Lucio Fulci) after buying it a few days ago, and now, after getting Day in the mail and actually watching it, I have to say, Day is everything Zombie was not.. from a decent cast, even with over the top performances, a good pace throughout, and an interesting screenplay.. It doesn't quite live up to Dawn of the Dead, but its a fine addition to George A. Romero's saga. The gore effects are right up there, and look pretty real.. the tension and breaking of the characters creates a sufficient atmosphere.. The only thing is that the ending is rather abrupt.. Some say its due to Romero not getting enough funding, and that may partly be true, but it brings up another question to mind.. She keeps having these dreams throughout, maybe she didn't survive at all? the end could be an expressed fantasy going over and over again in the mind of a zombie walking around somewhere.. Maybe this is all the last moments of her life flashing before her eyes before her change into the undead, and then the fantasy at the end going over and over again while her dead body wanders a new meaningless existence? In that sense, it could be like something right out of the Twilight Zone or Outer Limits.. and might be a final answer to the legacy, a final message about the world we live in and the zombies that now roam it, about the lost humanity that still exists, somewhere, buried deep within these 'undead' representations of ourselves.. This would also go right in with the story, and therefor, the abrupt ending might be more fitting than if it showed they escaped and got away.. Think about it, all of a sudden, we're treated as if they got away? Its pointed out in the film that the helicopter wasn't fueled up, and the zombies were there, so how did they escape? I don't think they did.. and in a way, if you can find this answer, if it really is what was meant, then the ending makes a lot more sense, atleast to me, as more a statement, the ultimate overrunning of oneself, the hero not surviving at all, but trapped, with one last fantasy, a small hope that didn't occur. I think, regardless of budget, Romero may have purposely decided it best to make the ending like this, without physically telling the watcher what it meant, therefor allowing a kind of interpretation, while also leaving certain clues inside the film itself as to what very well could have happened, and what did happen.The movie overall decided to go with a slower pacing, more built as an experiment in isolation, in the tradition of 'John Carpenter's The Thing', being ordered to continue something that may be futile.. in a sense, it reminds me a little of Re-Animator, but done to the style of George Romero.. To wrap it up, it may not be the best film, but its got it where it counts.. I liked it... and remembering Zombie, which should have stuck to the water, Romero proved once again he's on top of things, regardless of the limited minds not letting him do what he wanted to because of their lack of funding.. Maybe Romero will get to do the original script he had in mind in the new millenia, someone believing in his vision caughing up the money needed? I can hope..</fullReview>

		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A superb last installment in the "Living Dead" Trilogy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Run of the mill fans of the horror genre seem not to like this last movie in the "Living Dead" trilogy. Thoughtful viewers, especially, those that look beyond the blood and gore of this series, perhaps those who enjoy foreign films with subtitles, are more likely to appreciate this film.Unconventional as usual, the courageous, intelligent protagonist, is a female, towering above the other people. White males are, as in the rest of the series, not main heroic figures. This is a rarity in mainstream cinema and is a refreshing change.Day of the Dead brings new insight to the viewer about the Living Dead. We begin to see things a bit more from their point of view--that they are victims too, especially with the one experimental subject, who elicits sympathy, as he succeeds in remembering some aspects of his life before death. Much like the empathy we could feel for Frankenstein's monster when he delights in looking at and smelling a flower, we get a different viewpoint of a "monster".At this military base, the thoughtful viewer sees that the dumb bureaucratic military have become the villains as they stubbornly insist on carrying out the bizarre research, which no longer has any relevance to the future.There is much wise commentary on our society, if one is willing to see it. This is a movie that can be viewed more than once, and each time, it is possible to get something more out of it.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2001-06-06</date>
		<username>foolish_mortal</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Underrated</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I think this film is deeper than many people give it credit for. The talky bit that everyone dismisses as boring and badly acted contains a lot of interesting musings on civilisation and power; there's the irony of the humans trying to teach the zombies to be civilised by 'rewarding good behaviour, 'We can trick them into behaving, just like we were tricked into being being good little girls and boys' as Dr Logan says, while their own behaviour is increasingly deteriorating as the rewards for civilised behaviour are removed. I don't know if most people think this is trite or they just don't notice it but I never see it mentioned, anyway I thought it was rather Lord-of-the-Flies-esque. Besides that it's interesting to see Romero's female protagonists getting stronger with each movie (In 'Night', they hid in the basement or just whimpered pathetically; this one chops off her boyfriend's arm with a machete). If you look at the ratings this movie has received on this site you'll see that the group that favoured it most was young women, I wonder if that could be the reason. Tom Savini's special effects are generally agreed on to be excellent, but something else I don't see mentioned much is the soundtrack, which is very cool in an eighties sort of way, and much more involving than the ironic jingle in 'Dawn'. This all adds up to make it an enjoyable and intellectually interesting movie.</fullReview>

		<username>Daywalker</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Hundred millions of zombies cant be wrong..!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Day of the Dead" is one of the most emotional and philosophical horror movies ever made. Many people were disappointed about it, because they had expected a giant horror epic, another improvement towards the cult classic "Night of the living Dead" and the sensational "Dawn of the Dead". Unfortunately director George A. Romero only had a very little budget, so he had to produce a claustrophobic drama instead of a bombastic epic. As "Dawn of the Dead" took place in a big shopping center, "Day of the Dead" happened in a little bunker complex. The only shortcoming is that Romero hadnt used more of those atmospheric scenes from the city as he did it in the beginning. Nevertheless the third part is the best of the serial: the characters are well developed and remarkable, the unknown actors do a great job, whats not typical for the splatter genre. Especially Howard Sherman as trained zombie Bub, Richard Liberty as Dr. "Frankenstein" Logan or Josef Pilato as choleric Captain Rhodes are fantastic! The story is more elaborated and far-reaching than in the first two parts: as the predecessor was pretty action-packed, this movie is more melancholy and dramatic (the relationship beneath the persons, the hopelessness...) on the one side; on the other its much more macabre and grotesque (Frankensteins experiments with the zombies, sacrificing his own people in the state of emergency "in the name of science..."). Also the F/X are excellent: in "Dawn of the Dead" the faces of the actors were painted with grey make-up, in this movie the living dead are bleeding, wounded, rotten... At the box-offices this film was not very successful. Perhaps the people were sick from the most of those cheap and nasty zombie flicks, which were stragglers to the immense successful "Dawn...". But it doesnt change the fact that "Day..." is another masterpiece by George A. Romero and an undisputed milestone of the horror genre! (10/10)</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>what was Romero thinking?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>He created two of the best horror movies of all time, didn't he notice some of the few details that made them so good? 1) Identifiable, sympathyzing characters ("Ben" and "Barbara" from NIGHT, "Peter" and "Roger" from DAWN). Every character in DAY had some sort of flaw/s. The Jamaican guy was nice but talked too much, the mad scientist was a bad actor, the military leader was loud and dumb, the Mexican soldier was overacting, the other soldiers were mean and idiotic, the girl had so many "bad dreams" that you got confused on what was really happening in the story, and the whole Bub thing was silly and moronic. 2) Zombies. The zombies are supposed to be slow, quiet, moaning creatures that only attack when they're a few inches from you. The zombies in DAY shoot guns, growl loudly, swing their arms, drive cars, salute people, and just keep coming outta nowhere. Also, people like to see the zombies marching through out the towns, chasing after citizens, banging on locked doors, eating intestines and brains, and they like to see that throughout the whole movie. A parade of zombies appear in the very beginning, but quickly dissapear for a long time. A few more pop up here and there in the middle, but those are the loud, annoying ones that you keep wondering why they won't just kill. Then at the end they're everywhere, and it gets annoying to see the same hundred zombies chasing after one person. But overall, the zombies in this movie are AWOL. 3) Location. NIGHT took place in a quiet, woods town where spooky stuff happens all the time, and in a place familiar to where many Americans live. Same with DAWN, in an average suburban mall where you would least expect a horde of killer monsters to flock and kill. At least portions of those locations were bright and satirical. This whole underground thing that they're in, whether it's a salt mine, a records storage facility, or a missile silo, it's dark, lonely, claustrophobic, confined, and depressing. It's also no fun to watch millions of zombies attack people in.Romero should have stopped after DAWN, and then the series would be just fine. Or perhaps it was the production value, or the script. Perhaps the movie is a good concept with a bad delivery, which it is. Perhaps DAWN and DAY should follow NIGHT and be remade into more modern and manic productions. Then again, I heard the remake of NIGHT wasn't as good as the original.</fullReview>

I have seen countless movies, obviously, but DAY OF THE DEAD stands out as being one of the most memorable and coldly effective. It is outstanding and unique in that the main characters, while different on many levels, attempt to coexist while surrounded by an unfathomable threat. The undead disease has spread, and humans are outnumbered by nearly half a million-to-one. The feeling of claustrophobia and desperate survival infect the film, taking it over as the rot of both the zombies and the minds of the military survivors become threats. This film makes me ill, and it's supposed to. It is one of only a handful of movies that generate true unease and hostility. This film wants to hurt you. Take my advice and let it. 		<username>Sonatine97 (sonatine97@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Day follows Night and leaves Dawn for Dead!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'm probably in the minority here but I felt Day was streets ahead of Dawn in nearly every department, but was still way short on the first and best, Night.We all know Romero wanted to make a slightly better Day film than what finally materialised - but that wasn't his fault but down to a rather weak minded money conscious studio. However, what we do get is a very intelligent &amp; thought-provoking film where the zombies have finally won the battle and all that is left is to run (what the soldiers want to do) or stay &amp; understand (scientists viewpoint), with the gore being incidental to the story.The film is very "talky" which I quite liked; for once Romero decides not to spoon feed the audience with simplistic signposting for the next gore/action scene; the story of scientists &amp; soldiers living together while "Rome burns" is a good one, even more so if you want to see implacably opossed groups react with each other in impossible conditions.We have the conflicts, the plausible views, the experiments, the guinea pigs, the reasoning, the death threats and an overall sense of hopelessness. The temptation to give up on all this doom is so easy, with a helicopter readily available to take the scientists off to a remote island and spend what time they have left in some kind of normality.It's this kind of human psychology that was partially touched on in "Dawn" with the scientists discussing options &amp; reasons during the numerous &amp; chaotic TV interviews, but were never allowed to challenge the main story of escape &amp; consummerism.Day of the Dead hits many good notes. Romero restrains the guts until the very end, although some of the experiments are quite gruesome too. The acting also is generally quite good, especially from the lead female, Sara (Lori Cardille), who not only has to keep the scientists on track, pull the helicopter pilots into line against mutiny, but also has to watch out from any surprise attack from the sex starved soldiers she is supposedly working with.A lot of criticism was pointed at Pilato's dictatorial &amp; over-the-top Captain Rhodes. but I loved his performance as a soldier in conflict with the orders he is given(to protect &amp; assist a bunch of scientists) while also realising that the battle is already lost and that by staying where they are is a complete waste of time. He really does play the ugly part very well - and his comeuppance at the end seems rather justified.My favourite character though, is Richard Liberty's Dr. Logan (or Frankenstein). He looks a trifle eccentric but offers the film some sombre reasoning to a story that threatens to implode. The only downfalls are the claustrophobic surroundings and the rather "pleasing" ending. But for all that, Day of the Dead deserves to be held with distinction in the trilogy. Hopefully Romero will do a fourth, and bring the story to a proper close. ***/*****</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2000-06-02</date>
		<username>PhilR-7</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Shame about the budget...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Not much could live up to "Dawn of the Dead" as a classic zombie movie, and while with this one, Romero does his best with the resources he had at the time.This sequel has been much derided for its slow, talkative beginning. But I think it serves well as an idea of how people would interact in such a situation. We have a group of scientists and soldiers, who at the best of times would disagree on most things. In this situation however, they are isolated in the middle of a global crisis with no idea whether there really is anyone else out there who can help them.I can forgive this film for being ever so slightly boring during its first half, because there are some interesting conflicts going on between the characters. There is the military stance, where their only purpose is to carry out orders, and the scientific stance with the clearly mad "Dr Frankenstein" carrying out gruesome experiments trying to find a logic behind the phenomenon that has almost consumed the Earth.What really saves this film from being a mediocre schlock gore fest is the introduction of the "domesticated" zombie, Bub. Who would have thought that we could have any sympathy for a zombie? (Well, I did at least). It's a thought-provoking idea - could mankind really have overcome this horror through training them to behave (as the scientists believe) or through extermination (the military ethos)?Unfortunately, we never find out - as the zombie horde inevitably find their way into the compound, thanks to some typically human stupidity. And then Tom Savini's fantastic effects take hold and completely out-gross anything seen before. This is the main attraction in this movie without a doubt, and I think it's worth your patience during the first half.The film never really satisfies your need for some kind of closure, being the final part of a trilogy. I just wonder what Romero could have done with the budget required for the film he originally intended.Not brilliant, however it introduces some interesting ideas to the premise but unfortunately cannot deliver what we all wanted. Still worth a look if you like zombie movies.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2000-02-22</date>
		<username>krfx</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>3RD TIME IS A CHARM</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'm assuming that most who read this have already seen this great film. When it all comes down to it, Day of the Dead delivers on many levels. Lets face it, people watch or go see a zombie film mainly for one reason- the gory effects action. Tom Savini's make-up effects are utterly awesome! They look ultra-real, and that is where this film succeeds. Director George Romero blends action, gore, wit, and seriousness very smoothly. Many have slapped "Day" for being too talkative, but the dialogue is intelligent and has a very strict purpose. People in conflict under almost uncontrollable situations. The characters are a great blend of good and evil. Soldiers versus scientists. These two groups specifically create some very intense dialogue. It never becomes boring because everyone has something to say about the situation they are in. Vulgar, but often hilarious comments by the soldiers break up the everyday talk syndrome. Plus, the soldiers are so unsympathetic and arrogant that most viewers can't wait to see how they meet their bloody demise. And Romero sets this up with gusto, building to a RIP-roaring climax. The film's last half hour shows Savini's inventive genius with fx make-up. Romero had to slow the pace and let this movie breathe. If you truly enjoy zombie films and respect Romero, then re- evaluate this film. It is clearly the best of the dead series. I will surely take heat on this, but I'll explain."Night of the living dead" IS a true classic. And "Dawn of the dead" is also a classic. But I think the aspect that puts "Day" on top is its realistic make-up effects. And realism hits that crucial nerve of the audience. They are technically superior to the other two films. Now I realize that the effects in the first two films were as good as they could possibly get for that time.....but "Day" stands alone as a great, underrated, gory, witty, well acted and all around technically proficient film. Let's realize that we are not judging Oscars here. There simply is no better zombie film than "Day of the Dead". Its job is to entertain and disgust,....and it succeeds with flying colors.</fullReview>
	</review>

Perhaps it arrived at a time when familiarity had bred contempt. It's questionable philosophical insights may have bored many, but intrigued me. There is a great speech by the pilot John about god's judgement and a generally unnerving existential atmosphere about the ending of everything. Performances are uneven but there is a truly sad and touching one from the actor who played Bub. Direction is gripping and nerve shredding, Romero's earthy blue collar sensibilities not allowing for sentimentality and if it's necessary for a character to die then he or she will, regardless of audience sensibilities. If the film has a structural weakness then it is due to the fact that the zombies chase a bunch of bad guys we dislike, removing suspense to a certain extent.There is a power to his films in that the characters who behave unselfishly in risking themselves to help others are the ones who survive which suggests that man is not beyond hope. 		<shortDescription>Much slower paced than the others in the series</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>_Day_ is a lot more slowly paced than _Dawn_ or _Night of the Living Dead_; for a good third to a half of the movie, the only zombies around are securely chained to the wall, tied to hospital gurneys, or otherwise indisposed. The lion's share of the narrative goes towards exploring the tensions within a mismatched alliance - a group of eggheads alternately protected and bullied by a small detachment of soldiers. I'm not sure if it was just the copy I rented, but the editing appeared incredibly jerky. 		<username>Roberto Sonaglia (zappygun@iol.it)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The gloomiest of the three</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The last &amp; most philosophic of George A.Romero's zombie trilogy is usually considered the weakest. It surely is the gloomiest, slowest paced of the three &amp; too dialogued. It lacks the chills of horror classic "Night of the Living Dead" &amp; the rhythm and humor of its Dario Argento produced sequel. But in the end it's not the film Romero intended to do: budget limitations forced the writer/director to fill with words what he couldn't show. And even if there are too many words, they are Romero's best. The characters are real, the message clear: ghouls are the first step of an evolution. Man blasted himself. Violence brings violence. There is a lot of well-done gore, too. Quite as much as in "Dawn of the Dead". Praise go to Tom Savini, Master of Horror Effects. This movie came out the same year as Dan O'Bannon "Return of the Living Dead"(an unofficial sequel to Romero's masterpiece debut). The end of a trilogy and the beginning of a new one. A step in evolution for Living Dead related cinema.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>1999-08-24</date>
		<username>David Choi</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Substandard effort from George Romero still has some ingenious strengths.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I first saw this film when I was a high school frosh a couple of years ago (back in good old 1996). To this day, the images of rotting, undead cadavers mutilating their human cattle still torment my mind...DAY OF THE DEAD probably has to be the most repulsive, nauseating, and just plain disgusting graphic horror film I have ever seen! Even if this holds true, George A. Romero's really, really gruesome masterpiece, DAY OF THE DEAD is, at best, mediocre.The story is in a post apocalypse, where some of the "last" surviving members of the human race rush to find other lonesome survivors. For some reason, the zombies rule the Earth, and for some reason, these zombies forgot to set themselves up a hierarchy! The beleaguered human remnants consist of Sarah (Lori Cardille), a strong and intelligent scientist, and John (Terry Alexander), a pilot who just wants to get the hell out of the `shelters' that they have stuck themselves in. These people are the sane ones! Now...meet the insane survivors! There is Dr. Logan (Richard Liberty), a mentally insane scientist, and Captain Rhodes (Joe Pilato) as the even loonier and more belligerent marine.The scientists and military men quarrel for over one hour of this film, (as confirmed by Mr. Leonard Maltin himself) trying to fix their predicament. On one hand, the scientists hope to find a cure to "heal" the living dead. On the other hand, the marines want to make mincemeat out of these primitive zombies...DAY OF THE DEAD is absolutely slow moving, lacking the energy of DAWN OF THE DEAD and the suspense of NIGHT OF THE LIVING DEAD. Sure, there is a plethora of gross out moments, such as surgery, cannibalism, and lots of disembowelments, but DAY OF THE DEAD is unremittingly boring and it even produces ennui within the audiences. You cannot blame George A. Romero; he did his best with both a constrained budget and an abridged script. I am sure that even he had higher expectations of this film.Still, despite the fact that DAY OF THE DEAD is a weak follow up to DAWN OF THE DEAD, DAY OF THE DEAD has plenty of fine moments which earn this movie its few merits. The premise about the last (and lost) survivors being victimized by their own fears and paranoia is of interest. It produces a hypnotic effect as well. The claustrophobic atmosphere in DAY OF THE DEAD works remarkably well also. In this sequel, Romero wanted to illustrate a chilling, fearful vision of an apocalyptic world ruled by zombies. As shown in DAY OF THE DEAD, this perspective is a disturbing description of what the future may look like where zombies rule the surface and the surviving humans themselves may begin retrogressing into a barbaric-like state...Another attribute in DAY OF THE DEAD is the acting. The performances in this movie are for the most part, very good. Although the actors do have a ridiculous tendency to overact at times, the majority of the cast does a great job delineating honest characters. For once, it is a revelation how the peremptory Rhodes tries to take control of the situation...with the threat of violence. Sometimes, he holds his own cohorts at gunpoint...just so he can make message clear. Talk about the use of brute force! Also, the relationship between Dr. Logan and his "prized" zombie "Bub" (magnificently portrayed by Howard Sherman) is particularly interesting and even humorous. Out of the over thirty specimens Dr. Logan keeps as `scientific experiments,' he thinks of Bub as his most promising test subject. It is also quite compelling to watch Bub remember some of his human characteristics. Bub unusually shows signs of human memory.Furthermore, DAY OF THE DEAD shows the consequences of what would happen if people would rather fight and argue with each other than work together. As implausible as it may seem, mankind may someday succumb to his own fallacies. If people refuse to cooperate with each other, then they might as well sign their own individual death warrants!By the end of DAY OF THE DEAD is a particularly gut-wrenching, stomach churning, skin-ripping moment. The finale is needlessly disgusting. (POTENTIAL SPOILER!) Thanks to Tom Savini's gory special FX, the viewers are treated to an indelible moment where zombies literally rip a marine apart...limb by limb. Yes, these scenes of human dismemberment are quite sickening.DAY OF THE DEAD has shown a lot of promise but unfortunately, it has not lived up to many of the fans' expectations. In addition to being a very talkative film with plot holes galore, this movie contains an ending that lacks coherence...and sense. The cop-out ending especially defied all comprehension, though Romero probably wanted it to be ambiguous. I was left scratching my head as to wonder when did this event take place and why. (I will not tell you the ending, see it for yourself.)Unfortunately, the flaws in this film outweigh the assets. DAY OF THE DEAD is a daring movie with more than a nuance of nobility, but a rather slack pace and irrelevant plot elements happen to maul a wonderful concept. The idea is great; on the other hand, the story-line needs to add a hint of logic. DAY OF THE DEAD earns an `A' for effort. The product though will not be rated as leniently. Let's just say that DAWN OF THE DEAD is much better...RATING: ** out of ****.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>George A. Romero's "Dead" series seems to get better with every installment. 		<username>Ben Rowland</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The bloodiest of them all</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>No one will argue that "Day..." is the weakest movie in George Romero's impressive Living Dead trilogy, but the mood and atmosphere of the first two is not totally lost here. "Day of the Dead" finds a group of military survivalists marooned on an island military bunker, with all the world around them going to hell. The "good guys" are the only woman, her few friends, and a doctor who decides that the only way to survive this zombie crisis is to find ways to control them. He has his subject, "Bub", the one zombie you really feel sorry for. Complicating this is that rest of the men on the base, who are obnoxious, sexist, and totally unwilling to get along with the others. It is an internal war, with their lives hanging in the balance. If you thought "Dawn of the Dead" was violent, this movie has enough blood to make a surgeon toss his cookies. Tom Savini has obviously spares no expense at making this one of the most realisticly bloody and gory movies of his career. While the blood in "Dawn..." looked more like melted crayons, there is plenty of guts and gristle here. In one scene, the plant a shovel in a zombie's mouth and pop the top of his head off. The camera zooms on his eyes, which are still blinking. Most impressive of all is the makeup, with thousands of zombies made to look actually dead. Repulsive stuff, but a delight for fans of the series. The acting, as usual, is very bottom of the barrel, but it doesn't really matter. Entertainment all around.</fullReview>

		<date>1999-08-10</date>
		<username>Steevh</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Romero Doesn't Wrap It Up. Thank God.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Sorry to break a few hearts here, but 'Day...' is by far my favourite of Uncle George's Apocalypse trilogy. 'Night', while solid and scary still, suffers from over-exposure and familiarity through the hordes of slack rip-offs- and 'Dawn' I've never liked; overlong, washed-out cinematography and an allusion to the inhumanity of Man that is nowhere near as clever as it thinks it is.With 'Day of the Dead', Romero- forced to cut swathes from his rambling screenplay by budgetry constraints forced upon him- accidentally finds the elements that together will make one of the most intelligent, convincing and above all frightening movies of the 'eighties.To ignore the gore for a second; it's the characters that really impress. No one lets the side down, and the ensemble cast totally convince as the Last Survivors of Mankind. Trapped in their bunker, surrounded by the useless toys of warfare, finally beaten by their own dead; Lori Cardille, Joe Pilato and Terry Alexander should be applauded for the performances here- 'Day' is quite the most exceptionally acted film I've seen in a long while. There is an immense amount of tension- and most of it comes from the relationships between the characters... it's almost as if the living dead are secondary. Trust humanity to spend it's last days arguing.Romero's script- with all it's enforced lean structure, and claustrophobic dialogue really impresses. He originally wrote it big; almost epic. God alone knows how that would have turned out; but I get the feeling that Romero needed the strict editing the budget refusals gave him. As it stands, 'Day' has no pretensions above its station; and while it makes some very pertinent points about Man and his stupidity- it never once tries to provide answers. In the original screenplay, the zombie-plague was more-or-less over, and the dead were the new underclass... in their place, a part of society, categorized. We didn't need to see that in 1985, and we don't now. This neatly ends the trilogy, and leaves the audience as horrified, and questioning as they were when it began. And why should it be otherwise? To ask for an 'ending', a neat 'wrapping up' would undermine everything Romero strove to put on screen through three decades of the dead. Please George, we need no 'Twilight...'Those wanting bloody mayhem will find it here too- and it's rarely been done with more panache. 'Day of the Dead' is almost the perfect horror film; and horribly under-rated.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Tin Man-5</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Not as good, but it still packs a punch!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>One would think that there was only so much you could do with a story about undead zombies prowling around on the Earth and eradicating mankind; however, director George Romero manages to keep the plots different enough for them each to be distinct and effective. In Night of the Living Dead, a masterpiece and the definitive zombie film, the dead were just beginning to rise and seven people tried to survive the night boarded up in a farmhouse. In Dawn of the Dead, the dead's population has grown and had populated the whole planet, and four people lock themselves up in a shopping mall in an attempt to keep the zombies at bay. Now we have Day of the Dead. In it, the zombies have almost completely wiped out mankind (it's a ratio of 400,000 zombies to one human) and a group of military men and scientists are in an underground facility.It is here where different ideas lie to finish the zombies off. The military wants to blow them all away. The scientists want to figure out how to reverse the process, and another doctor thinks it is possible to domesticate the zombies. All of the humans are on the brink of nervous breakdown, and they're always at each others throats, especially Rhodes, the near-insane military officer. Things get interesting, zombies break loose, and it eventually ends up with the obligitary gore-fest....Dawn of the Dead was a tough act to follow, but Day manages to do pretty well. The storyline is different enough to really work well. Sure, it's not as good as the other two, but you've gotta expect that....not all sequels can be good. But I do think that it serves its purpose, in introducing new elements. The cast is exceptional (especially Bub, the "domesticated" zombie with the semi-automatic) and the horror is pulled off well...there's more gore in it than the previous two. I gripped my armchair with surprise and terror several times. While its talky first hour (in which the premise and the theories are explained...necessary stuff, but still, we want more zombies!) prevents it from being as good as the other two films, all that stuff is still interesting, and it builds up to an explosive ending that nicely caps the Zombie trilogy. Nice job, Mr. Romero!</fullReview>
	</review>

The atmosphere this movie creates chills me, the world is dead, power is off in the mainland, the town is deserted and noone living can be contacted. Its how WE were before the very first act of social behaviour happened. Bub is taught this, he gets rewarded for doing certain things and frankenstein comments "It is the bare beginnings of social behaviour"... Is this maybe how we started? until we were conditioned into who we are and as frankenstein put it "they can be tricked into being good little boys and girls, just like we were tricked into it on the promise of some reward...." all can be summed up in one line of dialogue Frankenstein "We have to do this its our only hope, civility MUST be rewarded... and if its not rewarded...theres no use for it, theres just no use for it at all" It is such an oppurtunity to reflect on what our world would be like without society and order and civil behaviour.... 		<date>2008-07-02</date>
		<username>Chris Gottschalk</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>"I'm running this monkey farm now Frankenstein and I wanna know what the f*** you're doing with my time!"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>George Romero started out his great series of zombie movies with Night of the Living Dead, which not only sparked the series, but sparked the entire zombie genre. Night of the Living Dead was extremely shocking for its time, and remains as a cult classic of the modern era. 10 years after Night of the Living Dead, George Romero directed Dawn of the Dead, which turned out to be a lot more amusing than its predecessor. Dawn of the Dead was filled with black humor, which made it or broke it for viewers. I personally didn't like the black humor in Dawn of the Dead, but it didn't ruin the film for me. Either way, Dawn of the Dead was very successful, and became an amazing sequel. The next sequel would be Day of the Dead. The "Dead" series in general is about zombies taking over the world, and the titles of the movies have to do with what stage the zombies are compared to the humans. In "Night" of the Living Dead the zombies are only terrorizing a small number of counties in rural Pensilvanya. In "Dawn" of the Dead, the zombies begin gaining the upper hand. Now, in "Day" of the Dead, the zombies have taken over the world entirely, forcing people to harbor up in underground army bases.I'm going to be honest with you, I wasn't expecting much out of Day of the Dead. It is usually looked at as the worst in the "Dead" series. Now that I have viewed Day of the Dead, I am not sure why no one likes it as much. Perhaps it is due to the fact that it wasn't made on a big budget like Dawn of the Dead, but didn't feel like a low budget film, just as Night of the Living Dead had. Perhaps it is due to the music score. Whatever the case may be, Day of the Dead is my personal favorite entry in Romero's series of zombie flicks. Day of the Dead is a superb film in my opinion. The cast is great. Joseph Pilato in particular. Pilato sets the mood for the movie really well; making the movie claustrophobic, intense, and rough. I didn't feel like a single cast member did a below average job. I liked the role of just about every member of the cast. I also thought that the script given to the characters was really well written and that the character development was amazing, although not as good as it was in Dawn of the Dead. Another great point of the movie is the pacing. Believe it or not, the movie actually takes a turn from the zombies for about 40 minutes. This is a positive in this movie, simply because it lets the viewer know the characters. Romero does this better than just about any director to date. He makes some of his movies purposely slow paced, so that he can allow superior character development. Also, when the movie finally allows the zombies into the picture again, it hits you like a brick wall. The movie takes a VERY hard left turn.One of the things that makes the left turn so easy to remember is the special effects done by Tom Savini. Tom Saivni had started out his gore FX career with Tom Savini when he had done Martin. He then preceded to work in Dawn of the Dead. Soon afterward, Savini began to take his own path into early 80s splatter films. His special effects just kept on getting better and better until we finally get to 1985. Day of the Dead has Tom Savini's best FX. Day of the Dead may very well have some of the best special effects I have ever seen in my entire life.Day of the Dead is a must see for any horror/zombie fan. If you are one of the select few horror fans who haven't dabbed into Romero's zombie movies, then you should. Day of the Dead is my personal favorite out of the series, and remains as my favorite zombie movie of all time.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>This and Diary are my favorite dead movies.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Day of the Dead would make a great stage play.There is no Hollywood gloss on this movie. It is very much a discussion between characters each dealing with their situation. Day even takes it one step further by developing the zombie to have human elements. The monster in this movie is not just something to be shot on mass, but something to think about. The movie is almost entirely character developing dialog in the small setting. It really could be performed as a wonderful stage play. The art direction in this movie is another high point. Though this movie was made in the 80's, the art direction still stands up today. Zombie makeup is applied to the smallest detail, and various other props, largely human bodies being ripped apart, and intestines are all very realistic. The movie is at many times, clever and insightful. Though for example, I very much enjoyed the 2nd dead movie, it was largely intelligent action with the odd conversation spot. This, the 3rd installment is almost all character dialog.So, if you like your movies full of gunplay, action, and flatline characters with no personality, you will not enjoy Day of the Dead. If you like your movies more intellectual, and appreciate that element in the horror setting, you will love this movie.</fullReview>

Unlike a lot of horror films in recent years, which are mindless and totally lacking in real suspense or likable characters, this one actually feels satisfying after it's over. 		<shortDescription>Screw me but I like this better then Dawn of the Dead</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>George A Romero continues the classic series of Zombie films with Day of the Dead. After a sucker punch of an opening we find the world has been overrun by the living dead, the cities are decaying and life appears to have been exterminated...completely.The only survivors we know of are holed up in a nuclear silo; a massive complex of dark caves that whilst sheltering them also imprisons them and these people are not happy campers. Strains between the factions in the group are coming to a head and order is breaking down with violent incidents and threats becoming the order of the day. The 'Darkest Day of Horror' is about to begin.George A Romero's 3rd part in the 'Dead Trilogy' is a surprisingly underrated addition to the series. It is less comic and significantly darker and more threatening than the classic and most loved zombie film Dawn of the Dead and this is possibly a big part of why it is was less well received. However just because it lacks the humour of its predecessor doesn't mean it's a bad film. Quite the contrary in fact, this is an excellent film.The acting is often criticised as being poor, I disagree. The characters, particularly the army goons, are brilliantly over the top, they are red blooded, under educated, meat eating thugs who are holed up in a cave with no outlets other than beating the everlasting (you know what) out of each other. I think they would be somewhat wired don't you? Lori Cardille's character Sarah is well played, Rhodes (Joe Pilato) the psycho captain is quite a comic relief at some points and leeringly threatening at others, and a outstanding performances come in the form of Dr Logan, AKA Frankenstein, (Richard Liberty as the mad scientist) and Bub (Howard Sherman as the living dead prodigy).The history of this film is well documented, Romero was woefully under-funded and had to savagely cut his original script down to the bone, this fact has seemingly aggravated many fans more than it did Romero himself (who incidentally rates 'Day' as his favourite of the trilogy) and consequentially gets panned as being the worst of the series. Bloody, violent and sharp as a morticians blade this film is in my opinion a worthy follow up to Dawn of the Dead though it is significantly different in tone.</fullReview>

		<username>BaronBl00d (baronbl00d@aol.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Quite Good</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Much has already been pointed out about the merits and negative aspects of Romero's third dead film. There is not much sense in beating a dead horse(unless it was a zombie horse of course). The Day of the Dead film is a good film in its own right. Most reviewers automatically make comparisons with it to Dawn of the Dead and Night of the Living Dead. It is a different kind of film than both of those. Night is a pure shocker and its whole goal is to shock with the unknown in a unrelenting manner. Dawn was a shocker but at a different level..you knew you were going to see blood and guts but maybe not at such a hyperbolic level. Dawn is also a thinking picture about the materialism of our society, but because of the great amounts of never-before seen gore it was laced with a great deal of black humour and sheer slapstick. Day, however, is what Dawn is without all the hyperbolic gore or the humour. It is a thinking picture about what a group of people, 12 I guess divided into scientists and military people, do while basically stuck in a fenced underground military installation. Easily the most humane of the three pictures, Day focusses on the way people react in a crisis with differing ways of looking at things, and shows the stress of having lived days, weeks, and months in the new order. At one point, there is a wonderful speech given by John about why man should stop with his petty bureaucracies, why man has brought the plague of the undead dead with his search for the "Truth" and answers of the universe. Day of the Dead is really a wonderful essay on human nature both good and bad...and showing that each has a different way at looking at things and a different breaking point. The cast of unknowns is pretty good. Lori Cardille does a fine job as the only female in the camp, and two shining performances come from Richard Liberty as "Frankenstein," a doctor known for his creative bloody experiments on the dead, and Howard Sherman as "Bub" the thinking ghoul. All in all a very good film when taken as an entity by itself. It doesn't pack the scares like NOTLD. It has some extremely gory moments(one body literally torn in half), but does not come close to reaching the hyperbolic heights of Dawn. Yet, it is a fine conclusion? to the Dead series by a very capable thought-provoking director named George Romero.</fullReview>

		<username>The_Movie_Cat</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>"Well, that's the trouble with the world, Sarah darling - people got different ideas concerning what they want out o' life"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Day of the Dead is an acceptable addition to Romero's zombie trilogy, if not an acceptable conclusion.I honestly believe that if this had been the middle film, bridging the gap between Night's low-key claustrophobia and Dawn's extreme violence and scale then it would have worked better. Not a bad film on it's own merits, it feels flat as the final part of a greater whole, where Dawn had one of the most magnificent ends I've ever seen. Who could forget Ken Foree in the 1978 edition ending a film with the nonchalant "Alright...", leaving the viewer confronted with the spectacle of consumer zombies marching to muzak? Satirical and undeniably brilliant. Day by comparison grounds to a halt and seems to run out of steam rather than end with Romero's trademark abruptness.The film is also guilty of the gore that the second had alluded to; here a zombie's eyes look left and right after his head has been severed from the mouth up, while a soldier is still conscious after having his legs torn from his body and his entrails ripped out. (Yelling "Choke on 'em!", no less) Similarly, when another soldier commits suicide by placing a handgun in his mouth we are shown his brains blowing out behind him. It is this late-80s commercial aspect that daunts, as this is the only film in the series that concludes with "soundtrack album available", a reminder that the film is chock-full with over loud dated incidentals and an end-titles song.The first film in the series, Night of the Living Dead, didn't work at first for me as it's somewhat stilted technique and ? Duane Jones and Karl Hardiman excepted ? amateurish acting led me to believe it was an exercise in high camp. Watched a second time I realised it was played straight, just not played very well. Looked at as a template, it shows the degeneration of the zombies and sheds light on the myth that Day is a humourless instalment. Night has the nasty zombies (though they're never referred to by that word in any of the films); the ones who use weapons, the zombies who stab through the heart with a trowel. Dawn, by far the greatest of the series, has the consumer zombies who stagger like ten pins, waiting to be knocked down. Day, conversely, has "Bub". Bub is a docile zombie who listens to Beethoven and salutes soldiers, a friend to the amusing Dr. Logan. Logan begins to give an actual psychological profile of the creatures, and medical study of their nature. He also sites the monsters as outnumbering humans by roughly "400,000 to one". Perhaps what the film lacks most of all is a cinematic vision. There's a nice wide shot in the opening moments on an island inhabited entirely by the zombies. This at least captures some of the aspect of Dawn, which really did convince on a minimal budget and with adroit editing that the entire world was under zombie regime. But after the island prologue we enter the claustrophobic, squat laboratory and it all becomes closed in. Claustrophobia is fine when you're under siege, but the threat is almost wholly absent here. In fact, the only time the cast are really in any danger from the undead (apart from the gross-out finale) is in Sarah's (Lori Cardille's) dream sequences. A film lacks tension if the only danger your characters are in is falling asleep and dreaming about it. Unless of course that film is Nightmare on Elm St., of which this one has connections with it's excess of blood.Though perhaps the real dangers come from within. We are "treated" to one-dimensional soldiers who govern the underground research centre, most notably Joseph Pilato, absolutely lousy as Captain Rose. Better is G. Howard Klar as Steele, who shows some humanity under the macho rhetoric. The picture also sees the latest, and least successful, in a line of black leads, with Terry Alexander as the West Indian John. This is commendable, though can also smack of tokenism, especially when John joins an Irish lead to form the two lazy characters that never want to work. The Irish character (Catchphrase: "Jesus, Mary and Joseph") is also a heavy drinker, while John wants to "soak up some sun on a beach"; though in fairness they are the two most likeable characters.An interesting theory is the notion that all three films could run concurrently. In Dawn we see a roadblock of rednecks like those in the first, and here there's a reference to all the shopping malls being closed. I often wondered what a fourth film in the series would be called, as the good titles seemed to be used up. Tea Time of the Dead, maybe? Between Half Past One and Five O'clock of the Dead? Yet it transpires that George A. Romero has mentioned in interviews that he'd consider a fourth, especially as "Day" wasn't quite the ending he'd envisaged due to budget cuts. Fan rumours currently suggest "Dusk" or "Twilight" as the likely options. Let's hope it materialises, a Dead saga for the new millennium.Finally, I noticed one or two of the zombies in this film were green. Maybe that's what inspired the people behind the 1990 colorised version of "Night of the Living Dead" ? rather stupidly making the zombies green with bright yellow hair!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>one of the best</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>this is the best and least understood of Romero's zombie films. The film is quite a bit darker than Dawn but not so grim as Night 68. I have never really considered either Day or Dawn to be direct sequels of Night 68, because they only deal with the same general idea. None of the same characters are involved and each situation is unique. Romero took the cold war as his theme in this movie - it is the fear of annihilation. In Dawn, Romero was pointing out a society so in a rush to consume material goods that it was devouring itself. Day picks up on that general idea and takes it to its conclusion - mankind has been driven from the world above. Day focuses quite a bit on the relationships of each of the characters to one another. It focuses on the factionalization that occurs in groups under great stress. Some people have not seen Day as the commentary on human group behavior that it is. Anyone who has read Maltin's so-called review knows that he probably hasn't even seen the movie! Another facet of Day is that the acting for a horror movie is excellent. There is a real focus on characterization in Day that is lacking from many horror films. Michael Gornick's cinematography is excellent. His close in, up front, intense style of filming is the way a horror movie should be shot. Thank goodness they decided to spend some cash on the zombie makeup this time around. The slapping of grey and blue makeup on tons of people just didn't cut it and was a real slight on Dawn technically. In Day each of the zombies has been crafted as an original and it shows. So this is a great, great horror flick that I highly recommend.</fullReview>

		<username>Everett Jones</username>
		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>Not Romero's best</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>George Romero's "Dead" movies, Night of the Living Dead and Dawn of the Dead, are perhaps best known for their graphic gore, but that the success of those films was due to more than just Romero's willingness to show blood-and-guts is confirmed by this movie, the third installment in his trilogy. Day of the Dead more than outdoes its predecessors when it comes to grossing out the audience, yet I nonetheless found it disappointing. As surprising as this might be for a low-budget horror flick, Day's fatal flaw is in the area of plot and characters. Romero may not have gotten Oscar-caliber performances in Dawn, but the performers were likable, and the story of their travails, though not exactly complex, was an involving adventure story. In the final Dead movie (as of this writing) on the other hand, the story never takes off. The characters spend almost the whole movie locked in an underground complex protected from the hordes of the undead. They are divided between scientists studying the zombies and soldiers assigned to protect them, plus two helicopter pilots. Of course, there is much conflict between the two groups as there is with the zombies. So far, this sounds like a good setup for a horror movie, but Romero botches the execution, probably due to an over ambitious desire to make a serious statement, which should not be a horror filmmaker's first priority. Romero should have gradually increased the tension between the characters, as in The Thing. Instead, they're screaming and pointing guns at each other almost from the very first scene. This dissipates all of the tension, and clearly prefigures the ending. There's very little real conflict, since the soldiers are depicted as complete bumbling fools. Romero lets the actors playing the soldiers overact shamelessly, and the dialog he writes for them is simply terrible. These guys are so annoying that I couldn't for them to get eaten. What's the point that Romero is trying to make here- that soldiers are bad? When, inevitably, the military turns on the scientists, it's for reasons that the scientist could have at least tried to explain. Another problem with the story of Day is the pacing.Dawn of the Dead may have had a relaxed pace, for which it has sometimes been criticized, but it worked because Romero began with the zombies overtaking civilization, relaxed the pace after the heroes barricaded themselves, and then finished off with a spectacular final attack by the zombies. Here, on the other hand, there's barely any action until the very end. That's means that dialog has to carry the weight up until the end, and neither Romero nor his actors are up to snuff.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Day of the Dreck</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Romero's 3rd installment of his flesh eating zombie series centers around a small group of soldiers and scientists holed up in an enormous underground facility. Apparently this facility was a holding area for all types of government records and miscellaneous storage. I do want to stress that the place really is enormous. The characters even have to take a slow moving elevator to get to the surface.This small band is left, assumingly via government orders, to figure out a way to stop the zombies. The soldiers in this group are impatient and none too happy with the arrangement. The lead scientist is enthusiastic and delighted with the opportunity and even manages to semi-domesticate one of the zombies.There is an unbelievable amount of tension in this cast of characters. And I really do mean unbelievable. The tension strains the viewers "willing suspension of disbelief." Yeah, and I realize what I'm saying in context of a movie centered around the dead coming back to life in search of a meal. From the first scenes to the final one, Romero's characters seem only to be able to communicate with each other through shouts and gun pointing. Every scene is a confrontation. As the film progresses, this "style of communication" gets very wearing and irritating to watch. There is no natural build up of tension towards the climax. Our heroes in Day of the Dead are three characters named Sarah, John and William. John, who speaks with Jamaican accent, flies the helicopter the group has, as for the other two, it is unclear what there function is at the bunker. Maybe civil servants? Sarah, who is essentially the lead, is a fairly typical heroine who consistently tries to make the moral/rational choice in amongst the chaos. William is an Irishman with a propensity for drinking, in fact he is constantly sipping booze throughout the film. It is unclear to me what his role in the group is other then to help out. A whiskey swilling Irishman and sassy Jamaican helicopter pilot don't really add much to the story. One gets the impression that Romero put them in the story to make it more interesting, but I don't think the characters add much other then to seem just out of place.As expected Tom Savini, the FX guy, really delivers. His work is one of the highlights. The gruesome scenes are just that and the gore is, well, gory. Some of scenes where the soldiers are being ripped apart look a tad fakey. Clearly, there is the actors head coming up from the floor, but it, generally, works. Savini seems to get better with each film he does.As the story climaxes in a predictable fashion, we get to see the psychotic captain's come-uppance. Well before this point, I found myself completely uninterested in any resolution the film was going to offer. I just wanted the shouting to stop.Night of the Living Dead is truly a brilliant film and holds its own not just among horror movies but among films in general. Dawn of the Dead is also quite a good movie although feels a little dated in places. It was a natural progression from Night of the Living Dead and presents a grim world overrun by zombies logically and realistically. With Day of the Dead, one wonders only one thing.What happened?</fullReview>

The violence is occasional, but when it comes, it comes, not making me forget how nasty some of those scenes were. But there's a tedium about this film, more time underground in the bunker lazing around, then out on the zombie ground, and because of this, it ultimately fails, although the crackpot scientist adds some humor to the whole affair which I liked, as well as the headstrong female, who joins forces with a Jamaican colleague of all people to continue the fight. A rather disappointment here, when stacked up against Dawn Of The Dead, let me just warn ya.</fullReview>

		<date>2010-01-20</date>
		<username>Bensch</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Romero Apocalypse Part III</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>After the flawless masterpieces "Night of the Living Dead" (1968) and "Dawn of the Dead" (1978), with which George A. Romero de facto created the modern Zombie film, "Day of the Dead" of 1985 is the final part to a trilogy that comes pretty damn close to perfection. (In the meanwhile, three further films have been made, but I still find it safe to call "Night", "Dawn" and this film a trilogy.) While "Day of the Dead" may not quite be as important and essential for motion picture history as two foregoing masterpieces, it is the perfect continuation of Romero's Living Dead saga, yet another great, dark and truly terrifying classic that must not be missed by anyone interested in Horror, and, as the predecessors, a clever social commentary about human behavior in isolated and extreme situations.The living dead have long outnumbered the living, who have to hide in order to survive; presumably 400,000 by one. A small group of scientists and military members struggle to survive, dwelling in an underground army bunker. The scientists, among them Sarah (Lori Cardille), the only (living) female resident of the complex, are trying to find solutions to the mysterious Zombie disease. Apart from the obvious threat by the menacing Zombie masses, there is rising tension between the scientists lead by the somewhat weird Dr. Logan (Richard Liberty), who is aptly nicknamed Frankenstein, and the military members under the command of the fascistic Capt. Rhodes (Joseph Pilato)? Mad science is one of my favorite Horror-topics, and I have therefore always liked this aspect about this film, in which the 'Frankenstein' character conducts some seriously demented experiments. As in "Night" and "Dawn", the Zombies are not the only menacing force in this film ? the tension between the survivors is in equal measures. As its predecessors, "Day of the Dead" is not merely a film about Zombies, but mainly one that explores the social behavior (and growing madness) of people surviving in isolated groups. Lori Cardille makes a very likable protagonist as Sarah, whereas Joe Pilato makes an utterly villainous and unlikable antagonist. As does "Dawn of the Dead", this (once again tremendously gory) film profits from the incredible make up and gore effects by master Tom Savini. The film is brilliantly shot in general, and the masses of grisly looking zombies and incredibly well-made gory outbursts are particularly impressive. The film has a great score, which intensifies the apocalyptic atmosphere. Overall, "Day of the Dead" isn't quite as essential a masterpiece as "Night of the Living Dead" and "Dawn of the Dead", but it's a perfect sequel to these two films, and surpasses almost any other film in the field. This is creepy, morbid, incredibly suspenseful and spectacularly gory entertainment that no lover of Horror should miss. Highly recommended!</fullReview>
	</review>
