		<username>TheMarquisDeSuave</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Talk about an underrated film!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Talk about a film that never gets the credit it deserves. When originally released, many Romero fans panned "Day of the Dead" because it featured none of the social commentary found in his earlier works. However, I strongly disagree, and in my mind this is better than "Dawn of the Dead" (its a close second to "Night" as my favorite of the trilogy). Personally, I always felt the supposed social commentary was a bit overstated in "Dawn of the Dead". I thought the fact it was set in a mall was more of a clever joke as opposed to an actual subtext. One also has to remember that Romero insists the casting of Duane Jones in "Night of the Living Dead" was simply because he was the best actor who auditioned, not to add a racial subtext. "Day of the Dead" does away with political allegory and focuses on sheer apocalyptic terror. Romero also explores the human condition, as this features possibly the strongest characters hes devised.The film is very well directed by Romero, who keeps everything at a quick pace. No other filmmaker can makes sequences in the daytime so frightening. It was also a good decision to keep it short ("Dawn of the Dead" is a classic, buts its overlong by at least a half hour). The acting is a bit over-the-top, but the characters are so well-developed the film manages to overcome this flaw. Also, this was one of the first 80s science fiction films to feature a tough heroine as the central protagonist. "Day of the Dead" is a film thats been underrated for far too long. (9/10)</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Highly flawed, interesting failure;</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Oh, Day of the Dead. It's like a sad love story in my life; a romance that could have been. But as it sometimes turns out, we were just too different.It went wrong from the beginning. As a quirky 13 year old movie geek who'd just realized that movies are the single reason to live, and that horror opened new worlds that tingled sensations in his soul and psyche. I saw DAY before knowing anything about what the purist call "authentic" horror, and certainly unaware of who this Romero guy and how the movie was part of a legendary trilogy. You can pretty much guess the reaction. My shocked eyes popped out upon seeing the (to me) insane amounts of gore, the action towards the end was thrilling for sure and the gloomy atmosphere really made me feel that there was a whole genre out there that I didn't understand, yet couldn't look away from. But of course, the main part of the movie barely kept me awake.Years and years later. As far as I'm concerned, I am a professional and qualified horror movie-buff. George A. Romero's importance to the genre is obvious to me. And surely, DAY can't be all that bad. Bad reviews tend to come out of misunderstandings, I figured, and my early experience of the film clearly must have been a result of my inexperienced age. So I finally see DAY again, happy about the fact that I watch it with my new eyes and with tons of knowledge in my backbone. But you know. The verdict basically stays the same.A few years and years later. I get my hands on a nice little DVD box-set of NIGHT, DAWN and DAY, entitled "Trilogy of the Dead" (suitable, since I couldn't really feel LAND as part of the series, even though I wanted to badly) and I feel... Day of the Dead. It's IMPOSSIBLE that you were all that bad.So I watch it again, digitally cleaned up and in 5.1 surround sound. And I must say........ the verdict stays the same. But I'm not dumb enough to miss the point of the film, and you can't blame Romero for not trying. I like most aspects of DAY. It's just that it doesn't work.Firstly, the idea is great. Following NIGHT and DAWN the planet's apocalyptic situation is grimmer than ever. I like how the misery of DAWN comes off as kindergarten in comparison to the wasteland of DAY; the story is also quite good, with a group of people roughly summing up the nature of humanity - but living underground, in the dark, suffering of decay. And finally, Romero's ideas about the zombie evolution, the ritual points of social criticism, the themes of life/death and madness/sanity, the philosophical undertones and the visual symbolism are all great. What's the problem? Execution. The film is roundabout 70 minutes of talking and whining. I'm sorry, you may call me too dumb to be patient, but I dig french new wave as much as I dig eurohorror - but to me, I can't say that the film is all that jazz. I don't know, if the dialogs would have been more intellectual or if the pace would have been evened out with some excitement and if the characters had been more involving; The actors are all doing a good job, but the characters are extremities and don't get any chance to properly interact. They just whine. Upon all of this, you might say that the film is pretentious with Pilato's demonized villain and the different martyrisms of the heroes - but I can by that.The finale comes on big like a wake-up call and it's no big surprise. George could have filled the first two thirds of the film with something else than just explanations of his points. He could have written a book, if that was his wish. Film requires a visual eye, combined with the dialogs. DAY is unbalanced and tragically flawed. Even so, I do love Romero's every intention with it.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A very brave film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Maybe I'm becoming a bit shallow with regards to the films I watch but I found myself wanting the good guys to survive in this one. Much as I'd like to believe that I am on a higher intellectual plain or am just plain cynical, I think I am just as susceptible to a good ride as the next person. So, I think I like this one the most out of all of George Romero's zombie movies. Towards the end, where the three main characters were trying to get out of the underground facility, I found myself thinking, "I hope they get out. I hope they don't die!" Maybe the character's weren't as one-dimensional as I thought they were (I mean, even if they were, I didn't mind that). Well, I didn't like the depiction of the soldiers representing the military presence, that just seemed to have no dimension at all. However, I say this is a brave film because, of course, there is very little violence for much of the movie. A lot of it is focused on experimentation, discovery and discussion. Romero seems to be just making his own movie and not caring what the fans think. He's having the patience to introduce the characters and develop them instead of rushing into murder everyone and that must surely be a sign of a great director. If the so-called "gore-hounds" (as Empire rag-mag calls them) want blood then maybe they should experience violence first hand instead of on a flat screen. I say, let it be a cult movie, it makes it all the better for that and leaves it almost untouched. Almost, of course, because as I write this, a remake is imminent. What is it with the film community at the moment? Oh, another George Romero remake? Terrific! Here's a lot of money! Who needs originality? What a waste of the tax payer's money. Another impressive facet of the film is the sub-plot of Bub the zombie. In 'Land of the Dead', the whole zombies evolving plot line seemed rushed and quite stupid. In 'Day of the Dead', however, the evolutionary steps that Bub takes are small and feasible (in a sense! Not forgetting of course that we are talking about zombies; fictional characters). It doesn't feel like Bub is suddenly understanding everything that is put in front of him (the razor, the book, the personal stereo) but he is remembering and that's much more interesting. Now, I'm going to be a hypocrite and talk about the violent bits. Don't get me wrong, I like the violent bits as much as anybody but there is a cut-off point, I feel (except for in 'Braindead', obviously). I liked the first zombie who stood in front of the Sun and we see that his lower jaw is missing. The zombie who got up from the operating table and let his insides fall on the floor was pretty cool as well. I liked the action bits and I can only commend Romero for not backing down to studio pressure because, by the sounds of things, had he done so it would have been their vision and not his and that's as big a nightmare as any you will get from this film.</fullReview>

		<date>2006-09-04</date>
		<username>movie-monster</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>An Overlooked Piece Of 80s Horror</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Day of the Dead was the first George A. Romero movie I ever saw. I was 13 years old. I think its fair to say it left a pretty big impression on me, and its a film I'll never forget.Day Of The Dead is the third and final film in George A. Romero's original Dead Trilogy, which began with Night Of The Living Dead (1968) and continued with Dawn Of The Dead (1978).Firstly, Day Of The Dead is a REALLY DARK movie. It takes Romero's gritty vision (a world populated by zombies) to an apocalyptic new level. While in Dawn, the characters stood a chance, in Day, things really do look hopeless. Mankind is outnumbered 400,000 to 1, and a group of soldiers and scientists are holed up in an old WWII bunker near Ft. Myers, Florida. One thing that surprises me about this movie is that George A. Romero continues to rate it as his favourite of the trilogy. I personally think Dawn is the better film, but Day should not be overlooked. Many people dismiss this effort because they feel its a poor ending to the trilogy. It was slammed by the majority of critics on its release in 1985, but now, 20 years on, people are giving it a second chance, possibly because of Romero's recent film, Land Of The Dead, and the number of zombie movies released over the past few years.Day is a claustrophobic movie, with almost the entire film set underground. The scenery is made up of dark, creepy caves, laboratories, and a maze of corridors. I found this a little disappointing at first, especially when the opening scene features a captivating and desolate image of Ft. Myers, the streets inhabited only by the dead. Romero uses this monotonous scenery to build the tension.In Day, the audience witnesses the breakdown of a miniaturised society. Most of the soldiers are aggressive, macho, testosterone-fuelled types, ignorant to the point of disbelief. The military viewpoint in this film is epitomised by Captain Rhodes, who is played by Joe Pilato. He is a power-crazed megalomaniac who has absolutely no consideration for the scientists, and it seems his only concern is leaving the bunker as soon as possible and saving himself. Pilato over-acts at times, and plays a very angry character, whose aggression occasionally gets the better of him, but he is the guy you love to hate, and when he meets his grisly demise, your left feeling no pity whatsoever. He also has some fantastic lines such as 'I'm running this monkey farm now, Frankenstein!! and 'You want me to salute that walking pile of puss? Salute my ass!'. His thoughts and ideas are devoid of logic and reason. This blindingly obvious, black and white, good and evil contrast between the scientists and soldiers is evident throughout the film, and does get a little tiresome.With this movie, Romero continues to avoid tired Hollywood clichs by casting a varied set of characters. Day features both a Jamaican and Irishman in prominent roles, and the lead character is a woman named Sarah, played by Lori Cardille (an idea perhaps inspired by Linda Hamilton's character in The Terminator (1984), who, coincidently, was also called Sarah). The head scientist Dr Frankenstien, as he is known, played brilliantly by the late Richard Liberty, is one of the films most memorable characters. He spends everyday alone in the lab, dissecting zombie 'specimens' and trying to find a cure for the epidemic, whilst shielding himself from the conflicts beneath the surface. It soon becomes apparent he is losing grip on his sanity.Later in the film, Dr. Frankenstein introduces 'Bub', one of his zombie subjects, who he has, to a certain extent, domesticated. He shows little or no desire to eat the scientists and shows some faint traces of 'civil' behaviour. When Captain Rhodes enters the room, Bub salutes him immediately. He harmlessly stares at books, listens to music and uses a telephone, eyes wide with fascination throughout. The audience is meant to feel somewhat sympathetic towards 'Bub', and these scenes could almost be classed as 'moving'.Ultimately, it is the characters' inability to co-operate on a basic level which leads to their downfall. As the zombies become less significant, the film begins to focus more on the human struggles. Once the tensions reach boiling point all hell breaks loose, as the dead enter the bunker. Cue the blood soaked finale, courtesy of Tom Savini.The effects in this movie are truly outstanding. Inspired by his days as a photographer in 'Nam, Savini unleashes some of the most gruesome and at times, utterly convincing scenes of carnage ever put to film. Towards the end, this film just gets too extreme, and is definitely not for the faint hearted. Remember, this was made long before CGI was even a glimmer in Hollywood's eye. Most of this stuff would still be considered shocking now, 2 decades on, and Savini sets standards that only he is probably capable of breaking. His attention to detail pays off too, with most of the zombies looking visibly decayed.What I like about this movie is that while the acting varies drastically from the mediocre to the outstanding, Romero's sense of atmosphere remains throughout. From an era when nuclear Armageddon may well have been just around the corner, no movie does 'apocalypse' quite like Day of The Dead. The image of mankind falling apart under this unimaginable pressure, is brought vividly to life. It maybe dated, but it stands out. This film deserves to be seen by hardcore horror fans and any fans of the zombie genre.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>My Favorite of the "Dead" Series</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In 1968, George A. Romero shocked moviegoers with a brutal, dark masterpiece known as "Night of the Living Dead". It's unheard of use of gore caused its name to be burned into the hearts of horror fans forever.Ten years later, Romero followed up his breakthrough smash with a film that would become even more well-known, "Dawn of the Dead". It's less-than-subtle social commentary and shockingly strong use of gore caused it to inflict fear just with the very mention of its name.In 1985, Romero delivered his third installment of the "Living Dead" series. Unlike the previous installments, it was met with very mixed reactions, most of them being quite harsh. But there were those who loved it, those who understood it, those GOT it. This controversial film was "Day of the Dead"."Day" returned to the dark, gritty formula used in "Night" that "Dawn" traded for an amusing mix of humor and candy-colored blood. "Day" is full of extreme gore and violence, loads of language, and sharp, cynical social commentary.The story follows a group of people living in an underground bunker, some of them optimistic scientists, some of them borderline-psychotic soldiers, and some of them happy-go-lucky civilians along for the ride. This leads to some serious clashes as the zombie epidemic engulfs the world above them. One scientist searches for a way to cure the "virus", one scientist yearns to discover what drives the zombies, the soldiers debate whether or not to let the scientists continue the experiments, and the civilians just want to find an island somewhere and live in peace.Romero's script, which underwent severe cutting due to budget problems, is actually quite well-written and thought out. While a lot of characters maybe a little flat and one-dimensional, the dialogue is sharp and the commentary is relentless.And now, my favorite part of the film: the gore. This is a gore-hound's wet dream, in all of its face-ripping, eye-gouging, finger-crunching, blood-squirting glory. It just has never been surpassed, not even by 1992's "Dead Alive", which delivered more than its fair share of gore, but was approached in much more comic, unrealistic fashion.In 2005, Romero delivered what will most likely be the final chapter of "Living Dead" series, "Land of the Dead". After seeing all of the installments, which I all adore for different reasons, I must say that "Day of the Dead" has endured as my favorite of the series and become, in my opinion, one of the greatest horror movies ever made.9/10</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Day of the Dead delivered what Romero has always been great at - great apocalyptic zombie action. Lots of gore and some really creepy scenes of zombies feasting upon their prey. Furthermore, the central part of the movie seemed to concentrate on the "good" scientists being in conflict with the "evil" military. It was more like an anti-war movie than a zombie flick.That was, in my mind, enough to ruin an otherwise decent zombie masher. I recommend seeing Romero's other efforts first.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Romero's Third Zombie Film Is Easily The Worst</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Day Of The Dead is Director George A. Romero's last zombie film to date, and it is easily his worst of the bunch. The military vs. scientist angle along with the Frankenstein sub-plot really are formulaic in execution. Despite, the much larger budget for this film, it looks like a very cheaply made film. It's poorly made from a technical standpoint also, especially with the abrupt, choppy editing. The screenplay is pretty much static, and the claustrophobic setting, minus the surprises and thrills from the first two films, makes things even worse. The emphasis in this film is on graphic gore, bad actors overall, and excessively mean villains. The conflicts, and their resolution, between the scientists and the military group offer us nothing new. This prevents us from identifying with anyone or anything. The killing of the zombies is often played for laughs, and doesn't play well with the more serious plot tone. Gone is most of the social commentary seen in earlier films, and any intended messages lack originality, coming across as heavy-handed. The film does have some creepy good gore scenes however, which are the reasons most viewers watch this stuff anyway. * of 4 stars.</fullReview>

		<username>mentalcritic</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>There ISN'T an Alien 3 syndrome...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Alien 3 is a critically reviled film because, unlike its two predecessors, one relying on dramatic tension while the other relies on action set pieces, there are frequent flat, boring, slow spots. A much better script had been known to exist for some time, and why it was jettisoned in favour of one that kills off half the cast in a perfunctory manner happens to be one of the greatest mysteries of the Hollywood system.How is this relevant to Day Of The Dead, I hear you ask? Well, like Alien 3, it follows on from an original that was very atmospheric, tense, and intelligent. Not having seen Dawn Of The Dead, I won't comment about whether that sequel was an improvement or not, but compared to the gritty darkness of Night Of The Living Dead, Day Of The Dead is just uneventful. The film starts with a group of scientists in a helicopter, flying into a deserted city to search for anyone who happens to still be alive. Upon beating a hasty retreat from the zombies, we soon learn that these scientists are locked in a bunker with a mob of angry soldiers who are getting more than just a little stir-crazy. This is where the film, unfortunately, starts to fall apart.Basically, we just don't give a damn about either the scientists or the soldiers - the former are a group of sad weaklings who let the soldiers walk all over them, and the latter are a bunch of complete b**tards whom we'd all be happy to take out and feed to the zombies ourselves. There is noone we feel any sympathy for in this chapter (perhaps this was intentional). Another problem with the film is that, up until the last twenty or so minutes, there is little to no action. The first film wasn't exactly an action blockbuster, but the film moved along at a pace that allowed the viewer to keep interested. Unfortunately, Day Of The Dead moves slightly faster than a concussed slug. During my viewing of the Region 4 DVD, I found myself activating the Time Remaining display quite a number of times, only to groan at there being way too much viewing time left. This is not a "slow, more deliberate pace", it is basically boring your audience, something that should never be done in the world of horror or action films.Anyway, if you want to see how the Dead series peters out (the ending was obviously cut in because the makers were running out of cash), then this is an excellent place to look. Let's hope that the long-rumoured Twilight Of The Dead project is a lot better than this.</fullReview>
	</review>

Among them, Lori Cardille in the lead (she's pretty in an intelligent way and quite good for someone without a ton of acting experience, much like the 4 leads in Dawn of the Dead), John Amplas (the fine lead from Romero's Martin), Terry Alexander and Jarlath Conroy as the civilian, helicopter driving members of the underground team and Sherman Howard and Richard Liberty as the main zombie and the team's nutty professor, respectively. The supporting soldiers do a good job but some of the central ones are frankly pretty hard to watch. Gary Klar chews some scenery as Steele but not too much and he can be tolerated. Joe Pilato goes over the top as Captain Rhodes several times and he really does take away from the movie at times in a character as important as the one that he plays but then Romero should have known better IMHO. Ralph Marrero as Rickles is *horribly* over the top and his performance is probably the single most regretable part of Day. In a film with so many dialogue heavy scenes, an unbalanced performance can throw off some important moments and it does in Day. All in all though, I recommend Day of the Dead and have it in my DVD collection.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2004-10-16</date>
		<username>mentalcritic</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>A misfire, but a creative misfire...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The American system as we know and love it has never been kind to independent, extreme material, and Day Of The Dead is an excellent example of this. George Romero didn't seem to believe in the MPAA ratings system, and preferred to shoot all the gore on half the budget. The problem here is that for what the film is trying to do, a bigger budget was really a good thing to have. However, the film's problems are not entirely limited to the budget. There is also a certain element lacking that no amount of money can cover up.Unlike the previous Dead films, Day Of The Dead has a serious problem building characters that the viewer can care about or sympathies with. The scientists seem incredibly dense, and the soldiers display rare combinations of stupidity with ignorance that some doctors in today's world can't seem to understand to not fit in with today's consumerist world. It's a pretty telling sign that the most memorable character in the film is a domesticated zombie that one of the scientists calls Bub.Another difference compared to previous Dead films is the ending, which is so abrupt and resolute that the audience should feel cheated. By contrast, Night's ending is set up by the rest of the film, where the massive efforts of the living are defeated by their paranoiac inability to cooperate. By comparison, you could divide Day Of The Dead into the last five minutes and everything else that happened beforehand, with a particularly uneducated film-goer never noticing that these pieces of footage are from the same project.I can't say I understand why Romero would consider this his best work. It is slow, poorly edited, and generally unpleasant. Nonetheless, it beats the hell out of many other zombie flicks, especially those supported by the Hollywood system, such as Resident Evil: Apocalypse. Or Resident Evil: We're Too Scared To Aim Above The Lowest Common Denominator, as it should have been called. The recent trend of fast-moving zombies is thankfully not started here. We're shown the right way to realize zombies, overwhelming the living by sheer numbers than any strength on their part. It's a social commentary - think of all the mindless sheep who support an inferior product and give the makers of that product leverage to overwhelm those who don't want it. In Romero's films in particular, it becomes something intellectual, as opposed to the sheer mindlessness of the MPAA's typical product.I gave Day Of The Dead seven out of ten. By the standards of other Dead films, it only deserves a five, but compared to the mainstream product, it is a masterpiece. It is enough to make me hope that the Twilight Of The Dead project everyone has been talking about for some time will eventually go ahead.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Gore don't equal Good</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In this movie Romero seems to be ripping off the very movies that ripped off his earlier, excellent efforts. Gone is any aspect of subtlety, or nuance, or commentary on society that the first two movies held. In lieu of dialogue we get screaming and yelling, with the occasional bloodletting thrown in to sate the audience-types who expect that kind of thing. Romero obviously wanted to wrap up the trilogy here, but he doesn't seem to have anything else in his mind so this comes across as a "finish it and get it out of the way" sort of approach, rather than anything that any deep thought went into.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>overrated</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I saw this movie for the first time a few months ago, and I was less than impressed. The acting is uniformly terrible, and the dialog is not particularly good and, at times, laughably bad. The basic premise and story are decent, but the interesting themes and ideas (the power struggles that occur at the breaking point of civilization) behind the movie are dealt with rather clumsily. The gore, which I hear was quite shocking for its time, is not that affecting today but is nonetheless well done. Overall, Day of the Dead's concept had potential but was badly executed (I recommend Ghosts of Mars instead of this movie since it has similar themes and is at least half-decent in terms of acting and direction). This movie is a disappointment since Night of the Living Dead was such a delight. I would normally give this movie about 4/10, but since it is a Zombie Movie, it earns a bonus point. 5/10</fullReview>

		<date>2004-11-27</date>
		<username>Nigel St. Buggering</username>
		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>A very long fall from lofty heights</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Night of the Living Dead" and "Dawn of the Dead" are, very rightly, legends in the annals of horror cinema. They are both extremely well made, at turns horrifying and humorous, and very clever in their use of the undead as social statement. That may be where "Day of the Dead" ultimately falls apart.I hadn't seen the trilogy in years, and the impending remake of "Dawn" spurred me to rewatch the three. The experience deepened my appreciation of the first two, confirming for me that they deserved their classic status. I think, though, that I was being far too kind to the third entry. This is a film with very little to say, and can't even find an interesting way to say it. There is no statement here, no social satire. Romero seems to be taking the concept very literally; rather than finding something new to say about the antagonists, he literally dissects them.The undead (nowhere in any of the films are they referred to as zombies, by the way) are being medically studied by an unethical and twitchy scientist in an attempt to "domesticate" them. The experiments take place in an underground shelter, supervised by a small band of soldiers, all of whom are portrayed as either wild-eyed psychos or shrieking hyenas; so much for complex character development. The lead researcher is also clearly missing a few aces from his deck, which leaves us with a pointless research assistant, an electronics engineer with a drinking habit, a Jamaican chopper pilot, and "the woman". She is apparently also engaged in some kind of research, but since we're never told what it is, she's simply "the woman", and her role in the screenplay seems to be to disagree with everyone. The characters cling to the single note they've been given, and never reveal additional facets. The one hint of humanity is found in "Bub", a ghoul on whom the scientist has been performing intelligence and memory tests in an attempt to make him more docile. It says something that the only moments of pathos in the film come from a dead guy.With no character development and little plot, we're left only to marvel at the effects, and they are indeed marvel-worthy. Tom Savini has really gone out on a severed limb here, and designed some of the most fiendishly ingenious prosthetics ever seen on film (although I wonder whether creatures who are already dead would really bleed that much). Not enough praise can be heaped on this work; it really is phenomenal.On the other end of the scale is the music score, which flat-out sucks. It's a tuneless, monotonous, synthesized drone that would be more at home in a bad high school romance flick than in a horror film. It's simply awful.Romero's recent announcement that there would be a fourth "Dead" film has fans abuzz with excitement, probably because they're engaging their selective memory and focusing on the first two excellent movies in the series. Maybe another viewing of this one would temper their enthusiasm a bit. This is the "Battle For the Planet of the Apes" of the Dead series.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Just further down the slippery slope</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's just occurred to me that while I think of myself as a fan of zombie films, that there are only three genre films that I actually enjoy: Night of the Living Dead (the original and remake), and Return of the Living Dead.These films all have something in common; they're taut. They pick a theme, stick to it, and pile it on to an inexorable conclusion. All other zombie films dawdle and meander annoyingly, and Day of the Dead is no exception.That's not to say that Day doesn't have a sense of inevitability about it. The whole plot progression is boringly predictable, and it's the boring part that sticks in the mind. I know what's going to happen, but I don't particularly care. Annoying people yell at each other, and I don't care. Ludicrous stereotypes over-act, and I don't care. Bub the Zombie moves his jaw from side to side, and I care a little. Then the live people take the screen again, and I go back to not giving a damn.We know what's going to happen, but it takes so long to get there that it's actually a relief when the live cast start meeting their sticky ends. I for one was cheering for the zombies, which up to a point is what I'm supposed to do. What I'm not supposed to want is for all of the living cast, including the ostensible sympathetic heroes, to be shredded. The rapidly tacked on brutally optimistic ending is a huge disappointment; I'd far rather find out what happened to Bub than the insipid nobodies whose fate I'm supposed to care about.This is a film that would have been far better left in the can, or better yet, as a script waiting for enough budget to afford actual actors and editing.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Appalling</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Day of the Dead takes everything that was wrong with Dawn of the Dead and amplifies it. The bad acting, the cheap feel, the predictable plot and the bad effects.The plot revolves around yet another group of survivors of a zombie epidemic: half of the group are military personnel (although I use the term loosely) and the other half are scientists and other miscellaneous people. The actors who played the soldiers either seem to have been told by the director to overplay their roles or are simply bad actors; regardless of which, every soldier is a racist, a bully, trigger-happy, stupid and contemptuous and suspicious of all of the scientists. Of the other members of the group there is a scientist (to whom the soldiers refer as "Frankenstein" again and again in a predictable reference to his working with zombies and corpses), some Irishman with a very dodgy accent who seems to have a limitless supply of alcohol despite references from him and other members that supplies are running low, a Jamaican who, despite being a pilot, refers to his helicopter as a "whirly-bird", another scientist whose role was lost on me and a woman who is the main protagonist. I found none of the characters likable whatsoever.The film starts off interestingly enough: the pilot lands his "whirly bird" in some town and the woman asks loudly if anyone's around in some city. I knew what would happen, loads of zombies started to descend upon the group and they took off quickly. While watching this, I thought how stupid the characters must be to think that would work as survivors are unlikely to be in such a location. Upon returning home, we have the familiar scene in all of Romero's films: the fortress with hordes of zombies trying to get in. Underground, we find out that the leader of the soldiers, Captain Rhodes, and his sidekicks cannot act. They are angry at being stuck in the bunker. After another thirty minutes of very little action and arguing and pointing guns at each other, they all decide to carry on as normal.Eventually, we learn that the scientist has been trying to domesticate the zombies and has discovered that they need not eat to live and that they can survive with no internal organs. I have to say, trying to explain zombies scientifically was a big mistake by the scriptwriters. The fact that dead people can walk around and inexplicably want to eat living people as opposed to themselves (living dead are fussy eaters apparently) needs a fair bit of suspension of logic from the viewer but having a scene with a zombie with no internal organs moving around and (in another scene) showing a severed head still moving is taking it too far I think.Anyway, the scientist gets the soldiers to bring him "specimens" (zombies) from some underground store (how they got them there in the first place is never explained, we're just told that they're running out of them and they'd have to get more from the surface eventually) and of course something goes wrong and some of the group get bitten by zombies. I have to say that even decaying zombie teeth seem to be able to bite off human flesh with remarkable ease, it's almost as if the group's bodies are made from silly putty.After more dilly-dallying, we learn that the scientist has made progress with a zombie he called "Bub" who is learning to use razors again. Captain Rhodes is angered that his men have died trying to help domesticate zombies when they should be killing them and then another boring argument ensues about how there isn't enough ammunition to kill them all. After Captain Rhodes refuses to salute "Bub" back, "Bub" (given an unloaded gun by the scientist) pulls the trigger on the Captain (because not saluting a zombie back is deserving of death you see) but as the gun is unloaded, nothing happens.We later learn that the scientist domesticated "Bub" by feeding it body parts from fallen soldiers. I really fail to see how such a stupid creature could distinguish between fellow zombie flesh and flesh from corpses already days old. The Captain (quite rightly) shoots the scientist for this unethical practice of feeding zombies fallen soldiers and then "Bub" inexplicably breaks his chains, sees the scientist dead, somehow knows that the Captain did it, gets hold of a gun, shoots the Captain, salutes him and then the Captain gets killed in one of the most unnecessarily grotesque scenes I have ever witnessed. The scenes of gore really add nothing to the film's plot - I know that zombies eat people and the earlier scenes of biting arms and throats were enough. I advise anyone watching this to skip these unnecessary scenes of gore.I forgot to mention that, yes, as in all Romero zombie films, the fortress is somehow penetrated by the zombies and they start invading in mass numbers. In this film it was done by a group member trying to kill himself who'd had his arm amputated earlier in the film.The soldiers all die and the woman, the pilot and the Irishman escape to some island. Before they get on the helicopter, we're treated to a fake unhappy ending which turns out to be a dream.The intelligent zombie premise was redone by Romero in a more ludicrous way in the almost equally bad Land of the Dead.The acting is appalling, the script terrible, most of the film was just pointless arguing and like Dawn of the Dead, the film has a nasty cheap feel to it. Avoid.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Schlock, but occasionally effective schlock.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The first thing you notice about "Day of the Dead" is its extremely low budget. Although that does help Romero to create a unique, depressing, claustrophobic atmosphere, it also tends to move this film towards the "schlock" category. Everything is poorly lit, which may be appropriate for this environment, but often makes it hard for us to clearly see all those celebrated gore scenes. And the effects are generally uneven; some of them are outstanding, but others (some disembowelings) look distractingly fake. I have to warn the gore-hounds that this movie isn't quite as gruesome as they might have heard; "Braindead" or "Hellraiser II" were INCOMPARABLY more sickening."Day of the Dead" is consistently unpredictable, and there are quite a few surprises. But the characters are unlikable and hard to identify with (that "Jamaican" guy was supposedly the most likable one, but I could hardly understand many of his lines) and the ending is disappointing and leaves you feeling cheated.</fullReview>

		<username>The_Void</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Dark and morbid climax to the Dead Trilogy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The third part of George Romero's masterpiece; The Dead Trilogy, is the darkest and most compact of the bunch, and while it never reaches the awesome highs of the previous two, it still stands on it's own as a more than passable zombie gorefest. Speaking of gore; this film has oodles! Because the film is darker and more unpleasant than the other two, the gore hits home more. Whether it be the nasty experiments of a man nicknamed "Dr Frankenstein" or the now legendary splatter soaked finale, you can always be sure that this film will have something up it's sleeve to shock and/or disgust you! The way that the trilogy has panned out is rather good, and George Romero has obviously put some thought into what would be the consequences of the events of Night of the Living Dead. This film takes into account the idea of humans not being able to co-operate with one another, which makes the people in the movie just as big a threat as the zombies. We follow 12 humans that are holed up in an old World War 2 bunker, and we see the threat that the humans cause themselves with the conflict between the scientific and military interests.Romero has shown his inventiveness with the make-up effects throughout this series. In Night of the Living Dead, the zombies were hardly decayed at all. By Dawn of the Dead, they were more bloody and more decayed, and by the time of this film; it would seem that effects guru Tom Savini has been given free licence to create some of the most disgusting effects ever to be seen in a zombie movie! The often green and white skinned living dead walk around with dried blood caked to their carcasses, limbs and jaws missing; you name it, these zombies have it! Naturally, it's the make-up and gore that are the stars of this movie, and the human stars take a back seat. It's a good job that's the case as well; as the actors here aren't up to much. This is almost a benefit to the movie; however, as it gives it that low budget feel that many movies fans, such as myself, love. There are some elements of the movie that don't work - seeing the talking zombie always makes me cringe - but on the whole, this is an invigorating and inventive zombie flick and while it isn't as great as the previous two; it's still pretty damn good.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Not My Favorite in the Series</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In the south of Florida, a group of scientists and military men are surrounded by the undead while they try to find a way to reverse the zombification process. But as the group faces the zombies on the outside, they also must face each other on the inside (not unlike the situation Romero returns to in "Land of the Dead"). Can a small group of humans survive when grossly outnumbered? Many consider this the best in the series. I don't. For me, that honor goes to "Dawn of the Dead" (the original). But I will say this is far better than "Land of the Dead", which relies on a very similar plot and comes across as weak in most categories. Frankly, Romero is out of ideas (which is evident if you look at his upcoming projects).I liked the zombies, and I must give credit to Greg Nicotero and Tom Savini for making some great gory monsters. The intestines getting ripped out, decapitations, and more -- everything that makes a zombie film worth watching. The lab scene where Dr. Logan (Richard Liberty) shows his experiments on mutilated corpses... oh, that's some great special effects.Lori Cardille played a good lead as Sarah. Yet another "final girl" archetype for the film students to study. Unlike much of horror, she was more strong-willed than sexy, but if anything this gives Romero a special kind of credibility, not having to rely on flashy or sexy actors to sell his ideas. Sherman Howard was also great as "Bub", the talking zombie. We see this type of zombie explored again in "Land of the Dead" (but not as well), and I think this is clearly the inspiration behind "Bud the Chud" (from one of my favorite films, "Chud II"), though I can find no blatant evidence for this.If you love zombie films, this is a must-see. From the opening scene that made me jump all the way to the gory conclusion, this is now a modern classic. Zombies today (2006-2007) are coming back into fashion, but let us never forget that the groundwork for them in their modern incarnation was really laid out by Romero. Pay some respect.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>You know, right when I think that I'm a fan of the Dead trilogy from Romero, I had the silliest notion the other day, I've never seen Day of the Dead. I bought Day of the Dead without seeing it and actually found myself loving this movie and seeing a possible inspiration for 28 Days Later. It's always interesting to see who did it first and George was way ahead of his time when he wrote and made this great film.Sara and three survivors, John, Bill and Miguel, land a chopper in deserted streets of Fort Myers to search for other possible survivors. They try to call people with a megaphone, but only attract hordes of undead. The group returns to their base, an underground army missile bunker near the Everglades. The group is revealed to be part of a military-supported scientific team assigned by the remnants of the government to study the zombie phenomenon in the hopes of finding a way of stopping or reversing the zombification process. But of course there are certain soldiers who are taking their jobs a little above and beyond their line of duty.Day of the Dead was a great conclusion before Romero continued his story with Land of the Dead, which is another movie I highly recommend. The sad part is, this would happen in real life if this situation was true and again makes for great writing.Another thing that I loved was I saw an inspiration for a movie I saw a few years ago called Fido where we got to see what a zombie who was made to fit back into society and we see in Day of the Dead a zombie that actually starts to remember his life before he died named Bubba. I loved that the scientist was like a father to him and that he was so quick to avenge whatever wrong was done. If this movie was made today, I would say Bubba deserves his own movie. The dead series are classics and way ahead of their time, not to mention that they molded the zombie genre. Day of the Dead is a great movie not to be missed and I think I may throw a Zombie party one night for a marathon, nothing says friendship than flesh eaters.8/10</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>How to make the perfect Trilogy ?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>How to make the perfect Trilogy ?...Well, ask Mr. Romero. Because he did it with his trilogy of the dead. And although many people say this third installment is a disgrace to Night and Dawn...they couldn't be more wrong. Although Day of the Dead is a known and famous movie, it might as well be the most underrated horror movie ever made. It deserves a lot more credit than it receives. Night of the Living Dead was a milestone in horror. Original, dared and one of the most influential movies ever made. Dawn of the Dead is pure perfection. Claustrophobic to the max and it gave a whole new definition to the word "gore". True, Day of the Dead doesn't do anything new but it gives a lot extra to the complete series. Romero manages to create a whole new sort of tension. In Night and Dawn the enemy were without a doubt the zombies. But in Day...you fear the survivors a lot more. The story : A small group of survivors (a few scientists and a couple of military men) are the last humans in a very wide area. When they fly the helicopter out to find others, their search never give any results. They are hidden in an underground base, well protected against the zombies who are dwelling around the area. While they all fear to become eaten, a doctor (who they call Frankenstein...) tries to train a few zombies. Although he has results ; the zombies begin to obey him; the others become impatient and want to get out of there before it's too late. The tension between the two groups becomes unbearable and pretty soon the zombies aren't the greatest danger anymore...The insanity of the survivors now is!!Day of the Dead was brought out in 1985. During this period a lot of zombie movies came out and that's probably a reason why this movie wasn't the big success that its predecessors were. Wrongly if you ask me. Day of the Dead has a good story, lots of action and gore and good acting performances by unknown actors. Especially the cruel Rhodes, commander of the military men, gives away a flawless performance. The insanity due to the unbearable pressure really comes out in his role. I regret to say it but, this is in fact Romero's last fantastic movie. After 1985, he made inferior films like the Monkey Shines and the Dark Half but these titles never lived up to his earlier work. It is rumored that there might come a fourth film about the dead but nothing is confirmed yet. Let's keep our fingers crossed, because there's no discussion about it...Romero is at his best when he's surrounded by the living dead...</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Another great slice of juicy zombie mayhem from Romero.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There are some who say that Romero lost his way with his third 'dead' movie, Day of the Dead; I disagree. Whilst it is true that the first hour may be a little heavy on the talking and rather light on the old blood and guts for some fans, the director sure delivers the goods in a rip-roaring final act in which he ramps up the action and allows top FX man Tom Savini to paint the screen red with some astoundingly gruesome gore.Personally, I love the slow burn approach Romero takes with this with chapter in his long-running tale of world domination by zombies; it gives you a chance to get to know the characters before they have their legs ripped off, and that's a good thing, methinks. This time around, the action takes place in an underground complex where a bunch of scientists and some nasty soldiers attempt to figure out what to do about the whole 'walking dead' situation.Crazy Dr. Logan (Richard Liberty) reckons he can domesticate the shambling corpses: teach them how to perform simple tasks and not to crave human flesh. However, Captain Rhodes (Joe Pilato) isn't too impressed by this idea and, when he discovers that the mad doctor has been rewarding his star pupil, Bub, (Howard Sherman) with tasty tit-bits cut from recently deceased soldiers, he decides it is time for he and his men to leave.With no further use for the civilians (with the exception of a helicopter pilot), the soldiers act ruthlessly, killing some and leaving others to be eaten by the scientific 'specimens' kept in the complex's cave system. But their plans are scuppered when a delirious soldier, the still-living victim of a zombie bite, opens the gates to the hordes of the undead? Featuring one of the greatest zombies of all time in the form of Bub, and showcasing some of Savini's career highlights, Day of the Dead is unmissable entertainment for those who like their movie monsters to smell bad, walk slow and have really bad teeth. The film starts brilliantly with a search party landing in a town, searching for survivors, but finding maggoty flesh-eaters are the only inhabitants. The next hour is spent introducing the characters and building the tension (with the occasional mutilated corpse or graphic gut-spill thrown in to remind you that this is still a Romero film).The final half-an-hour or so is packed with full-on over-the-top carnage, culminating in a couple of the nastiest death scenes it has been my pleasure to witness.It is a real shame that twenty years after this excellent film's release, Romero gave us a fourth 'dead' film which genuinely saw him losing his way: Land of the Dead was a real disappointment for many fans of the series. Let's hope that the fifth film (Diary of the Dead, due out soon) sees him back on form.</fullReview>

First of all it's too over-the-top, explicit and cartoon-ish to be taken realistically. Second of all the Dead trilogy is, strictly speaking, supposed to take place all in one timezone (despite the two decades' difference between filming). But in the original "Night of the Living Dead" there was only a handful of foul language -- in "Dawn" there were a couple uses of the "F-word." Here, there are about 100 -- practically every other line in the movie contains profane language.Not a problem. I own and love "Reservoir Dogs," "GoodFellas," "Pulp Fiction," "Raging Bull," etc. -- these all have very strong language (more so than "Day"). But the difference is those films are realistic and the language, in context, isn't offensive -- to cram it into a very immature, silly, childish zombie picture seems deliberately stupid, especially considering the fact that there was barely ANY bad language in the first two films and they are both far superior!The acting is quite awful, the dialogue is very weak (it always was in every installment), the zombie gore is the best here and if that's all you're interested in, then this is your piece of cake.However for me I prefer the suspense and ingenuity of the original and the social satire of the second, even if "Dawn" is a bit overrated. "Day of the Dead" is just a huge disappointment, a crudely drawn, over-the-top and silly, stupid zombie flick that's no better than all the rip-offs that were made during the '70s and '80s.2.5/5</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The Least of the Four Deads</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Day of the Dead is entertaining and gory and violent, but it's more of a sit-down drama involving the study of these monsters that we've seen living humans blindly and instinctively fighting against in the Night and Dawn films. Instead of continuing the same basic non-stop action gorefest, Romero is original enough to take us through an analysis of the situation, unlike the Terminator films, any other slasher series, even the Lord of the Rings trilogy. All of the most interesting unexplained details are drawn out in this one. The problem is, the acting is very very bad, and there is not enough action like in Night and Dawn to deflect it. These clumsy, apprehensive actors are given the first 50 minutes of exposition time, where they have to barely make it through extensive scenes of dialogue.The film is not completely devoid of action or gore, like I disclaimed in my first sentence, but that only comes in the very climax. With this third installment in the beloved living dead trilogy, Romero is much more interested in psychological connections between humans and zombies, analyzing the inner psyche of a walking corpse, etc.I like how this series of films is spread out into one per decade, and fulfilling every characteristic of cinema in that particular decade. Night of the Living Dead was purely iconic new wave 1960s film-making, Dawn of the Dead was a celebration of every trait you can think of in glossy, tinsel 1970s cinema, and this one, Day of the Dead, plays up the soundtrack, the hairstyles, the speech, the special effects, and the cinematography of the 1980s. It's certainly the worst of the four living dead films, Land of the Dead being a nice improvement, but it's enjoyable enough.</fullReview>

		<username>Michael DeZubiria (wppispam2013@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Music soothes even the savage zombie.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The third and final installment in George Romero's Living Dead trilogy is surprising in that it comes so close to being a dismal, depressing close to one of the most famous horror film series' ever made. True, the movie sucks pretty badly and pretty consistently except for the last 20 minutes or so, but that last 20 minutes come on pretty strong to save the rest of the movie. Strife among characters has been a major element in the series since the beginning, but in Day of the Dead the characters are so antagonistic toward each other that the movie almost becomes unwatchable. We have gone from an uncomfortable group fighting a small but quickly growing hoard of zombies in Night of the Living Dead to a group of people who mostly get along except for a herd of bikers with an inexplicable urge to kill them in Dawn of the Dead to this, a bunch of military guys and some scientists in an underground bunker, so suspicious and angry at each other that there are fingers on triggers pointed at other living people throughout the majority of the movie. And here I was thinking that something that threatens the entire human race might tend to bring the remaining survivors together, regardless of their differences. If Independence Day taught me a single thing, I thought that was it. I could see, for example, Michael Moore and Dick Cheney desperately fighting for each other's survival if they were, like the people in Day of the Dead, outnumbered by walking cannibalistic zombies 400,000 to 1. Not this movie.Interestingly, the movie starts out really well and ends really well, kind of like a really good song often does. The opening of the movie is a brief but fascinating tour of the streets of a city taken over by the dead. Here was something a little strange that happened while I was watching it ? during this opening sequence, I was reminded of the description of the deserted Jerusalem's Lot in the beginning of Stephen King's novel ?Salem's Lot, which I am currently re-reading because it was mentioned at the end of Wolves of the Calla, the fifth installment in Stephen King's Dark Tower series. The ghost town of ?Salem's Lot is described as though it were suddenly deserted, even with a transaction rung up in a local store and $50 cash in the register, untouched. The town in Day of the Dead is overrun with garbage and rotting carcasses of cars, alligators for some reason roam free, and cash blows down the street like leaves. Later in the movie, a copy of ?Salem's Lot actually makes a cameo appearance. Weird!Anyway, the new things that happen in Day of the Dead (these things take their sweet time in emerging from the endless human bickering, by the way) deal mostly with the psychology of the zombies. For that reason, the vast majority of the movie is clinical and dull. The setting is an underground bunker, and we are forced to sit through scene after scene of a scientist conducting numerous tests on a captive zombie, which could be a pretty interesting character study (how often do you actually get this close to a gory horror movie villain?), except that the military guys are just down the hallway sulking and threatening to kill the hard-working doctor if he doesn't come up with some results and fast. I could see the captain getting mad at the scientist for suggesting an evolutionary link between humans and reptiles, but what else did he want the guy to do, sit around and mope with him and his buddies? The smartest thing this captain Rhodes ever does in the movie is call the doctor `Frankenstein,' although Rhodes probably doesn't even know he's right that Mary Shelley's Frankenstein is the doctor, not the monster.We find out eventually that the scientists big plan is to find some way to control the zombies, although the obvious obstacle is that this plan would require close and fairly extensive contact and treatment of millions of zombies to put them all under control. Not exactly a plan that lies within their power, but the doctor correctly concludes that the only way to win against the zombies is to make them behave. There are also some clever scenes that ponder about the role of God in this whole spectacle. One character speculates that God has risen the undead as a way to punish the human race for our sins, hinting to the audience that we better straighten out and live holy lives. What a message to get from a horror movie! But then, a couple minutes later, the assertion is made that the only way to make the zombies behave is to trick them into thinking there's some reward coming to them for good behavior, the way the rest of humanity has been tricked. So much for the God message?They exercise control over the zombies (one zombie, anyway) mostly through the use of classical conditioning, positive reinforcement, and attempts at model behavior (`How are we going to set an example for them if we behave barbarically ourselves?'), which results in a clever role for one of the zombies and the unexpected result that one of them becomes a protagonist. The caves at the end mark the beginning of the best portion of the movie. They're very effective atmospherically, despite the goofy, bright red haunted-house-ride lighting. The gore is largely absent from most of the middle of the movie, since this is the portion that's so preoccupied with the humans arguing with each other, but the gore at the beginning and at the end has improved massively from the last two movies. Some scenes were a little too realistic. Given recent events in the Middle East, I seem to have lost what little appetite I may ever have had for beheading scenes, and one such scene in the movie takes place while a man is screaming, his scream becoming more and more high-pitched as his vocal cords are stretched and severed. I'm only 25 and I think I'm too old for this.There is an interesting scene in which one of the characters is bitten on the arm by a zombie and they try to save him by cutting his arm off, supposedly before the zombie poison can get to the rest of his body and take him over. That was clever, I hadn't seen that in any of the other movies. This character, incidentally, is the one who ultimately goes on to make an `escape attempt,' which probably was the single event that saved the movie from the Oblivion of the Ridiculous, a realm populated by countless thousands of horror films. I won't go into details, but his attempt to escape is the moment that the movie starts getting good. Sadly, since there are only about 15 minutes left in the movie by this point, it doesn't have time to move in the right direction for very long. Ah well?</fullReview>

		<date>2008-05-21</date>
		<username>Paul Andrews (poolandrews@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>One of my favourite films, absolutely brilliant from start to finish.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Day of the Dead starts as things look bleak for the few remaining human survivors hold up in a fourteen mile long underground silo, the Earth has now all but been taken over by flesh eating zombies as they outnumber the living 400,000 to 1. A team of civilian scientists look for answers, they look for cures, they look for a way to eradicate the zombie problem while the Military soldiers assigned to assist them grow ever more impatient. Tensions rise, the two groups have differing ideas &amp; the mission starts to fall apart as ammunition, food &amp; people start to run out. They are trapped, with millions of zombies outside waiting to eat them they must find a solution &amp; send them back to the grave for good...Written &amp; directed by George A. Romero &amp; following on from Night of the Living Dead (1968) &amp; Dawn of the Dead (1978) how would Romero approach Day of the Dead in order to equal or even better two of the greatest horror films of all time? Romero's original script was quite different, it's available on-line &amp; I have read it &amp; while it's not bad I am glad that it was this Day of the Dead that got made. Because the budget was slashed in half at the last minute when Romero refused to make an 'R' rated film he had to scale his vision &amp; script down although ultimately we will never know how that original script would have turned out if it had been made. I will just get on with it &amp; say it now, I love Day of the Dead &amp; I consider it one of the best horror films ever made. Period. Dawn of the Dead is also one of the best films ever made &amp; while that went for over-the-top comic book style action &amp; splatter (nothing wrong with that at all) Day of the Dead is a largely character driven film &amp; Romero's script is brilliant in that respect. Each character has a real edge, the dangerously near the edge Military soldiers including the hilariously foul mouthed Steele to their intense leader Captain Rhodes who just seems to explode with a terrifying rage to the sympathetic civilian scientists caught in the middle doing their best to the simply crazy Dr. Logan nicknamed Frankenstein who has just plain lost it. There are some instantly quotable lines of dialogue here, some of it's actually quite funny &amp; for me it was totally believable. Of course if your looking for splatter then Romero &amp; special make-up effects man Tom Savini don't disappoint with one gruesome blood soaked flesh ripping set-piece after another. Back in the late 80's when I was a young teen &amp; when Day of the Dead was released here in the UK on a horrible cut, murky 'so dark you can't see what's happening', pan &amp; scan VHS tape I got my mum to rent it for me &amp; I loved it back then for the extreme gore, top quality special effects &amp; filthy language, now I'm older &amp; hopefully wiser I can appreciate the superb script, the character's, the message about living for the moment &amp; the wonderful speech about keeping useless records &amp; statistics &amp; I can both admire &amp; enjoy the film on even more levels than just a simple blood bath. One of my favourite films of all time &amp; a film which I love even more every time I watch it.Director Romero does a good job here, personally I think Day of the Dead is his most accomplished film, I would probably say that the original Dawn of the Dead is the slightly better film but you really can't improve upon perfection can you? I love the opening sequence set in the deserted city (filmed at Fort Myers &amp; Sanibel Island in Florida) which soon fills up with hordes of the living dead, a wonderfully evocative start which sets the scene brilliantly in no time at all. Apparently Day of the Dead didn't do too well at the box-office, according to the IMDb it had a budget of $3,500,000 making $1,700,000 during it's first weekend &amp; ending up with $5,800,000 when it's theatrical run finished which surely means it made a profit? The gore scenes &amp; effects are superb, I would rate Day of the Dead as one of the goriest films ever made &amp; I have seen a lot of gory films. I once met Tom Savini at some convention &amp; asked him what was the make-up effect he was most proud of &amp; he said the Major Cooper corpse on the table in Logan's laboratory where it's a body with the face cut off &amp; the brain is just there twitching. I didn't argue with him, the effects are top notch.Technically the film is good, although the opening sequence was shot in Florida the majority of the rest of the film was shot in Pittsburgh in Pennsylvania. Another aspect of Day of the Dead which I think is outstanding is the score by John Harrison, it's maybe my all time favourite horror film score. The acting is superb from a great cast, I am genuinely surprised more of the cast in this haven't gone to do more things.Day of the Dead is one of my all time favourite films &amp; it's as simple &amp; straight forward as that. Zombies, some serious gore, a fantastic script that's sometimes as bleak as the subject &amp; a cool soundtrack, what else can I say? Followed by the vastly inferior Land of the Dead (2005) &amp; Diary of the Dead (2007) while the recent Day of the Dead (2008) claims to be a remake but isn't except in name only.</fullReview>

		<username>Theo Robertson</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Might Have Worked With A Better Script And Actors</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I remember when 28 DAYS LATER came out a lot of die hard horror fans decried Danny Boyle's movie as a rip off from DAY OF THE DEAD . I did have some reservations about the second half of 28 DAYS LATER , namely some quite illogical plot holes but since I'd never seen George A Romero's movie I couldn't compare and contrast . I eventually saw it last night and could see where the critics are coming from . They are both very similar movies but my criticisms are slightly different DAY comes with the set up of a scientific team who are trying to analyse the zombies being stuck in an underground bunker alongside a military unit . There's no plot holes in this scenario unlike the ones 28 DAYS LATER suffered from since the characters have all known each other a considerable length of time . There's also no unlikely " rainbow coalition " of the military unit either , the soldiers led by Captain Rhodes are , with one notable exception , entirely white something that Boyle blundered with by casting several actors from different ethnic backgrounds in his movie which strained credibility somewhat since no British Army unit is that cosmopolitan . There is also a problem with Romero's casting and that is none of Rhodes men resemble soldiers , they all look too old and too fat . It's not helped by the fact that none of them could act their way out of a paper bag either I'm afraid that the more I think about it the more I realise that if you're watching a Romero movie you have to suffer the bad with the good and nearly always one of the worst aspects with a Romero movie is the totally poor acting . THE CRAZIES suffered with this and after seeing DAWN OF THE DEAD again I noticed how bad much of the cast were . DAY keeps up the tradition . The military unit were bad but we're also treated to an Irishman whose accent is anything but Irish and a Jamican who I'm certain has never been outside of New England . Oh and if you're casting a Latino it might be a good idea to cast someone who looks like he might be Latino . Just having characters calling him racist names perhaps isn't enough . The actor playing Salazar might have a Spanish sounding name but that's not enough in my view . Did anyone else think Eric Roberts should have been cast as Rhodes ? The story itself did have intriguing possibilities but the script doesn't make the most of them . In this sort of scenario we're reminded of Conrad's HEART OF DARKNESS something 28 DAYS LATER utilised to much greater effect but here as many people have mentioned Romero seems far too interested in gore rather than intelligent story telling , and boy is this a gory film . I actually felt quite sick when the guts fell out of the zombie on the table and where Captain Rhodes met his end . I suppose Romero shouldn't be judged too harshly because in the mid 1980s gore was the primary selling point of a horror film but it is very frustrating watching DAY OF THE DEAD 20 years later , noticing so much potential in the story and noticing much of the potential has been wasted for the sake of stomach churning visual effects , and it's not like Romero isn't capable of better since DAWN OF THE DEAD featured incisive social commentary alongside splatter effects Before anyone asks I haven't seen LAND OF THE DEAD but there's no doubt in my mind that this is the weakest of the DEAD series . If you enjoyed any other of the movies in the franchise then I'm afraid you might be somewhat disappointed with this . I know I was</fullReview>
	</review>
	<review>
		<date>2009-03-08</date>
		<username>Michael_Elliott</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Third in the Series</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Day of the Dead (1985) ** (out of 4) Romero's third film in his (then) trilogy has a group of scientist and military personal held up in an underground bunker where they try and figure out the widespread zombie outbreak above them. Even after Land and Diary this remains my least favorite in Romero's zombie films but I must admit that I enjoyed it somewhat more on this viewing, which must have been my fourth or fifth. Thare are many, many flaws with this film but there's no denying that the special effects by Tom Savini are downright breathtaking. The biggest flaw with the film is the screenplay but I guess you can't blame Romero too much since this was a watered down version of his original idea. In case you haven't heard, the film was originally going to be a three-hour zombie film about the end of the world but the budget was cut and what we ended up with was only a fraction of what the director wanted. That's easy to see because when you watch the film you can't help but think there should be a lot more than what you're actually seeing. What I do blame Romero for are the characters here who I found rather weak. I didn't have that much of a problem with the scientists but I didn't care for any of the military men. I found them all to be rather childish, annoying and I just couldn't care for any of them. I'm sure Romero didn't want us liking them but making them so annoying was a major mistake in my opinion. The performances are a mixed bag but I thought Lori Cardille was good in the main and only female role. I also enjoyed Sherman Howard (Howard Sherman in the film) as Bub, the zombie who is being educated. Bub doesn't have any dialogue in the film but you can't help but feel he's the most all around character just with the facial gestures given to him by the actor. Joseph Pilato on the other hand gave a very weak performance. I have never been able to accept him in the role and to me he comes off as giving a weak Nicholson-like performance. The final twenty-minutes of the movie are downright brutal and rather breathtaking because of the masterful gore effects by Savini. I must say the zombies here do look better than in the previous two films and they certainly cause as much havoc. There are countless memorable scenes dealing with the gore but one of my favorites is when the zombie sits up from the table only to have his guts fall out. In the end this film is really a mixed bag but I still think there are more flaws than anything else.</fullReview>
	</review>
